Received 
      22nd February 2018
    First published on 16th August 2019AbstractThe replacement of inorganic semiconductors with molecule-based compounds for applications in current-to-light conversion has led to a significant increase in interdisciplinary collaborations worldwide, affording new improved organic-light emitting diodes (OLEDs) ripe for commercial applications, as well as light-emitting electrochemical cells (LECs) that have recently started to head to the market. This review highlights the role that transition metal coordination complexes (TMCs) have played in advancing the field of molecular electronics, from early conception to the advanced development of several polypyridyl complexes currently pursued for both OLED and LEC concepts. In this context, the design and synthesis of Ir(III), Pt(II), Cu(I) and Ag(I) complexes as the emissive components of OLEDs and LECs are thoughtfully presented. We discuss how molecular design is pivotal for fine-tuning color and optimizing power efficiencies, highlighting the key roles of the metal, cyclometalate, and ancillary polypyridyl ligands. We provide insight into the strategies exploited for the development of new, improved emitters and their fabrication into OLEDs/LECs with high external quantum efficiencies and stabilities. In addition, we have surveyed the remarkable photophysical properties of third generation TMCs capable of undergoing thermally activated delayed fluorescence (TADF). Since previous reviews of TADF materials are strongly biased towards organic-based systems, this overview compliments other synopses of light emitting TADF materials. Finally, we shed light onto the conceptual challenges that still need to be overcome to advance the rational design of TMC-based TADF emitters with tunable ligands and the subsequent fabrication of OLEDs/LECs, which are tailor-made for each specific application.

1 Introduction to artificial lighting: motivation and importance
At the turn on the 20th century, one of the major scientific breakthroughs that has impacted the everyday lives of humans on a global scale was the discovery of solid-state lighting (SSL). In this respect, SSL is based on the concept of electroluminescence, where light is produced by the radiative deactivation of excitons generated as a result of efficient electron–hole recombination in the bulk of a semiconducting material (see Sections 1.1 and 1.2).1–15 Underpinning this technology are thin-film and multi-layered electroluminescent devices comprising of either organic and/or inorganic semiconducting materials.1 At an applied bias, both holes and electrons are injected and transported through the bulk and eventually recombine forming an exciton that relaxes radiatively, yielding visible light. Hence, in contrast to traditional lighting devices where light is a byproduct, SSL allows for significantly reduced heat generation, as well as enhanced luminous efficiency. In addition, SSL provides excellent stability, brightness and light point sources of different colors.2,3 Traditionally, SSL technology is divided into light-emitting diodes (LEDs), organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs), and light-emitting electrochemical cells (LECs), Fig. 1. Light-emitting diodes are small point sources made of a thin-film of inorganic semiconductors combining several elements that include indium, gallium, phosphorous and nitrogen, as well as color converters.1,4,5
 Fig. 1  Schematic representation of the three main SSL technologies. Reproduced from ref. 16 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2018. 
Color convertors include the so-called inorganic phosphors doped with rare-earth metals like cerium, yttrium, or organic down-converters. In the case of the latter, organic/inorganic materials have been developed which are more commonly referred to as hybrid LEDs.6 Owing to their high luminous efficiency and stability, LEDs are now routinely implemented for screen backlighting, and home illumination, automotive, traffic signaling, advertising as well as merely decorative applications.1,9 In fact, the worldwide cost of artificial lighting for the aforementioned applications is currently estimated to be over 100 billion €, which is expected to increase by a further 30% in 2020.17–19 Although only 0.1% of the worlds lighting is currently supplied by SSL the rapid development of this technology has led to an exponential growth that over the next few years will result in a larger share of the market place, as traditional lighting sources, such as compact fluorescent lamps (CFLs) and incandescent light bulbs are slowly phased out.16 In this respect, the market for SSL has seen a 40-fold increase in installed lamps, since their debut in 2001,20 which contributes to more than 2.8 billion USD in energy savings.21 Thanks to the high efficiency and luminescent properties already achieved in LEDs, the subsequent increase in their stabilities now promises exciting opportunities with respect to the future globalization of this technology. The DOE states that by 2025, LEDs will produce at least half of the electric light in the US and even more globally.22 In 2016, the Centre for Industrial Studies (CSIL) estimated that the worldwide consumption of LED lighting fixtures was worth 41.8 billion USD, encompassing almost 47% of the world total consumption, corresponding to a growth of 20%.23 In this respect, LED lighting fixtures contributed to just 5–6% of the total lighting fixtures market in 2008–2009, yet this share is forecasted to grow by up to 67% in 2021.23 This change is largely attributed to the significant increase in luminous efficiencies which are expected to reach 150–180 lm W−1 by 2020, considerably outperforming all mainstream conventional lamp technologies (Fig. 2).21
 Fig. 2  Comparison of annual electricity consumption for residential, industrial and outdoor applications in 2010 and 2015. Reproduced from ref. 19 with permission from the http://Energy.Gov. 
What could be more desirable for commercial applications than a significant increase in luminous lifetime of up to 20000 h, with a lumen maintenance value >90%, which in the longer term has the potential to reach at least 50000 h? Furthermore, current CFLs contain an average of 5 mg of mercury per bulb. This is an environmental concern and leads us to conclude that SSL, not only offers increased power efficiency, but is also attractive for the replacement of older, less ‘green’ sources of lighting.20 Despite these advantages, current LEDs suffer from several constraints which include the blue hazard, light quality, temperature dependence, blue pollution, voltage sensitivity, high price and as point light sources they are difficult to use for applications that require a spherical field. In addition, such significant market growth requires careful consideration of the materials and resources used, as well as how they are interlinked, since the overall design of the product significantly influences the fate of the components at its end-of-use, or end-of-lifetime. In this context, OLEDs and LECs (Fig. 3) are emerging as interesting alternatives to LEDs, since organic semiconductors like small molecules, polymers and/or coordination complexes are responsible for carrier injection, carrier transport and emission. Despite the fact that both LECs and OLEDs are less mature than LEDs, they offer complementary technology that is particularly attractive for stylistic and large area applications, as well as for enhanced color quality (see Section 1.4).10 While efficient OLEDs comprise of multilayered architectures prepared via chemical vapor deposition and rigorous encapsulation,10 LECs consist of single- or double-layered devices fabricated using solvent-based, up-scalable techniques.11 Even though OLEDs are currently used as displays in small handheld applications and prototype TVs, high manufacturing costs have limited their dissemination into the lighting market. As an alternative, LECs offer the easy fabrication of moderately performing devices based on 3D shaped substrates that include kitchen forks,12 wearable fibers13 and light-emitting paper.14 Additionally, a record efficiency of 99.2 cd A−1 has now been reported, paving the way forward for practical applications.15
 Fig. 3  LED lamps require less power than older light sources for emitting light. Efficiency is also referred to as luminous flux (measured in lumen) per unit added power (measured in W). Approximately one quarter of the world's electricity consumption is used for lighting applications; highly energy-efficient LED lamps contribute to the energy conservation of the Earth's resources. Reproduced from ref. 31 with permission from the http://Scientific.Net, copyright 2015. 
Common to both concepts, the synthetic versatility of polypyridyl ligands renders them attractive for the development of luminescent transition metal complexes (TMCs) for applications as the emissive components of both OLEDs and LECs. In the following chapters we provide an overview of all of the families of polypyridyl complexes designed and optimized within the paradigm of SSL, highlighting the most suitable device structures, as well as the best emissive complexes reported to-date.
1.1 Historical evolution of artificial lighting
In contrast to the 20th century, where light came from incandescent light bulbs, most artificial light in the 21st century will arise from the emission of LEDs. This prediction was made when the Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences awarded the 2014 Nobel Prize for Physics to three scientists acknowledged for the invention of efficient white LEDs. By extending cheap and affordable lighting sources,24 LEDs have the potential to improve the quality of human life on a global scale. For example, about 1.5 billion people in the world do not currently have access to electricity grids, a problem that could be solved via the large-scale commercialization of LEDs. As shown in Fig. 3, LEDs operate more efficiently than all other lighting sources, reaching 300 lm W−1, when compared to just 70 lm W−1 for their fluorescence counterparts. Although the most well-known applications of LEDs are light bulbs, other uses have included the removal of pollutants from water and air.25 Improvements in the robustness of LEDs are evidenced by the exponential relationship between the durability of an incident light bulb, florescent lamp and LED namely at, 103, 104, and 105 h, respectively. This is one of the key factors that has contributed to the large-scale commercialization of LEDs, which have now flooded the market as the microelectronic components of displays for smart televisions, computers, laptops, mobile phones and cameras. In fact, many homes and vehicles around the world now contain LED technology.2,3,10,11,14,15
The events that have led to the discovery of LEDs can be traced back to limelight or Drummond light, the stage lighting first used in old fashioned theatres and music halls. Limelight, produced by placing an oxyhydrogen flame inside a cylinder of calcium oxide or quicklime, is now recognized as being the first form of solid-state non-electrical lighting. It was first discovered by Drummond in 1826 and its bright whiteness is recognizable for a distance of up to 68 miles.26 Remarkably, this phenomenon precedes the first incident filament lamp discovered by Thomas Edison and Joseph Swan in 1879. However, Edison's filament lamps were preferred since they produced light without any unpleasant smells or smoke. In this respect, Edison constructed the first metal filaments from carbonized paper which he later replaced by fibers fabricated from Japanese bamboo. The discovery of the aforementioned light sources was soon followed by the observation of low-pressure discharge in gases, a discovery attributed to Peter Cooper Hewitt, the first inventor of colored light, originating from a mercury vapor lamp in 1901.27 A notable development in the broadening of the emission spectrum of these lamps then came about due to the parallel discovery of high-pressure gas-discharge. This phenomenon subsequently led to the discovery of the first low pressure metal vapor lamps that contained sodium metal, which like mercury also produce a visible glow. Although the patent for Hewitt's initial invention was purchased by General Electric in 1914, the first commercial low-pressure mercury and fluorescent lamps were not produced until the 1930's by rival, Westinghouse Electric Corporation.28 The emission in these lamps originates from phosphors coated onto the inner walls of a glass tube that affords a low-pressure discharge when irradiated with trace amounts of mercury vapor at low pressure, that causes the lamp to glow.29,30 In recent years, the discovery and study of solid-state inorganic and organic LEDs has rapidly accelerated their commercial applications, rendering them now smarter, lower cost and more environmentally friendly alternatives to traditional incandescent lights. Despite these advantages, one of the most important goals in this field is to tune their emissive properties, to achieve color temperatures (CT) no higher than 3000 K. In this respect, a higher CT rating generally means an increase in blue emission. The discovery of strong and stable blue emitters for OLEDs remains a challenge that has provided significant momentum for the intense research activity in this field over the past decade. In addition, prolonged exposure to blue light can harm vision by damaging the retina of the eye and blue light can also suppress the secretion of melatonin that in turn disturbs circadian rhythm. However, if the aforementioned constraints can be overcome, optimizing performance and reliability issues, then the outlook for these materials, well beyond 2019, should be very “bright”.
Semiconductor LEDs are currently the major components of small indicators that are typically found in games consoles, remote controls, as well as the interior lighting of cars. More importantly, they have been identified as key components for the fabrication of new generations of lighting and display technologies.
Electroluminescence was first discovered by Round in 1907, however Losev is accredited with the discovery of the first LED.32 In 1927 he patented a “light relay”, the first reference to LED-based optical communications, when he discovered that silicon zinc oxide and silicon carbide crystal diodes emit light when electrical currents are passed through them.33 Losev proposed that the nature of this emission is non-thermal and attributed it to the diode action of the LED. In 1929, he stated in a patent that LEDs could be used as detectors in optical relays for telegraphic and telephone communications, and as such is recognized as the first person to realize the true potential of LEDs for the advancement of telecommunications.34 The first practical visible spectrum LED comprising of gallium arsenide phosphide (GaAsP) was discovered by Nick Holonyak, Jr. in the early 1960's, who subsequently employed this red LED into numeric and alphanumeric displays, as well as indicator lights.35 For his singular impact on the progress of semiconductor LED technology, he is recognized as “the father of the LED”. Following Holonyak's pioneering work, research progress in this field rapidly accelerated, leading to the discovery of hundreds of subsequent emitters for LEDs.
Briefly, SSL based on organic materials was established in 1953 requiring very high voltages to observe emission of light from a crystalline sample.36 In 1987 Tang and Van Slyke reported the first OLED with a sandwich-like architecture, a few hundreds of nanometers in thickness, using emissive and injection layers prepared by physical vapor deposition.37 The charge injection and electron–hole recombination processes at the interface of the bilayer were decoupled, achieving luminous efficiencies of ca. 1.5 lm W−1 at only 10 V. In 1990, Burroughes et al. achieved another breakthrough by preparing sandwich-like OLEDs using solvent-based deposition techniques with conjugated polymers.38 Low external quantum efficiencies (EQEs) of 0.05% at an applied bias of 14 V were obtained due to the low electron–hole recombination rate caused by the selection of electrodes and the lack of charge injection/blocking layers. Tang and Van Slyke subsequently enhanced the electroluminescence efficiency by employing a multi-layered device architecture with several layers devoted solely to charge injection, charge transport, and charge recombination processes.39–41 Advances in device design were accompanied by the development of new phosphorescent materials that include TMCs,42 as well as the realization of light out-coupling architectures that have subsequently enhanced device efficiencies.43,44 Today, OLEDs still suffer from several shortcomings that include: (i) the use of air-unstable electrodes; (ii) multi-layered architectures to reach high efficiencies; (iii) the fabrication in dust-free environments; (iv) device encapsulation to avoid O2 and moisture and (v) low fabrication success of 3D shaped substrates.
In parallel to the multi-layered approach followed in OLEDs, Pei and co-workers proposed the use of conjugated polymer emitters together with mobile ions that can assist with charge injection and electrochemical doping (p and n zones) in single layer devices.45 The resulting devices feature low turn-on voltages (2.0–3.0 V), reaching 200 cd m−2 and EQEs of up to 2%.45 This family of thin-film SSL devices represent early examples of LEC technology. In these devices, the active layer is a blend of an emitter (conjugated polymer, TMC, small molecules, QDs, etc.) with a reversible electrochemistry and high photoluminescence quantum yields in thin films, that is blended with an ionic electrolyte (ion-doped polyethylene oxide or ionic liquid). Upon applying a bias, the ions are polarized by the external electric field, allowing charge injection from air-stable electrodes. The charge injection is further stabilized by the ions leading to three well-defined zones: p-, n- and a neutral or intrinsic zone from which the light is emitted. In fact, LECs show interesting electrical features that include (i) bipolar behavior with a rectification ratio near unity; (ii) full tolerance compared to the thickness of the active layer and (iii) a fairly high tolerance to the type of air-stable electrodes employed.
One year after the first reported LEC, Lee and co-workers demonstrated LECs comprising of cationic Ru(II) complexes.46 Furthermore, in 2004, Slinker and co-workers advanced the field of LECs further by successfully employing ionic Ir(III) complexes.47 This seminal work has led to the discovery of interesting, state-of-the-art TMCs, employed in devices which present stabilities of thousands of hours, as well as high luminance and efficiency levels that are comprehensively summarized in this review. Despite the type of emitter, the best performing LECs still emit in the yellow region. In fact, red LECs show low efficiencies that are limited by the poor photoluminescence features of this class of emitters. Furthermore, blue LECs possess low stabilities caused by the degradation of the emitter due to the charge injection process and/or a population of degradative excited states upon electron–hole recombination that include the triplet metal centered states (MC) in Ir(III) complexes. Finally, the quality of the active layer morphology is the key to providing homogeneous doped regions which strongly depend on the nature of the emitter/electrolyte, deposition techniques and electrodes employed. These limitations led to the discovery of the first LECs based on Cu(I) complexes in 2005,48 which have attracted enormous interest over the past 5 years.49–54 In parallel, small molecules have been applied mainly to develop red LECs55–59 and thermally activated delayed fluorescence (TADF) LECs.60,61 Finally, a few QDs and/or NPs as emitters for LECs have also been reported.62,63 Nevertheless, there is still plenty of scope to enhance device performance by optimizing the electrolyte for the different emitters, as well as device architectures and driving conditions.11,64
1.2 The mechanism of OLEDs and LECs
As previously mentioned, all LED architectures comprise at least three main layers: an n-type that contains a surplus of negative charges, a p-type having an insufficient number of electrons and a third active, or emissive layer sandwiched in between. In such systems, when an electric voltage is applied, the resulting electrons move from the negative n-type to the positive p-type layers and radiative recombination together with the holes leads to light emission. For both direct and indirect band gap semiconductors, the color of the emitted light depends on the band gap, defined as energy difference between the valence and conduction bands. Increasing the mole fraction of the lighter elements in the composition of semiconductor LEDs results in a larger band gap, affording emitted photons with higher energies. The increase in efficiency coupled with a decrease in power savings for LEDs, when compared to more traditional light sources, is highlighted in Fig. 4.65 It is apparent that replacing older light sources with LED technology will significantly reduce energy consumption, which in turn makes these materials more eco-friendly and cost effective, Table 1.
 Fig. 4  Potential energy savings and efficiency improvements of white-LEDs compared to traditional light sources. Reproduced from ref. 65 with permission from the Optical Society of United States, copyright 2008. 
Table 1 Summary of the global benefits of solid-state lighting technology over a 10 year period66

As previously highlighted, the basic working mode of an OLED is based on the concept of electroluminescence. To realize their full commercial promise, the correct balance between the density and mobility of the charge carriers coupled with a good match between the energies of the HOMO and LUMO frontier orbitals of the emissive layers, holes, and electron transport layers must be achieved.67 The crucial components of an OLED device are highlighted in Fig. 11, in which HTL and ETL are the hole and transport layers and HIL and EIL are the hole and electron injector layers respectively.68 The efficiency of an OLED is measured in terms of figures of merit (FOM), which are influenced by a number of factors that include the composition of the electrodes, chemical composition of the emissive material, hole and transport layers, the class of polymer host, purity of the emissive compound, thickness of the transport layers, as well as the degree of air contamination.
In contrast, LECs operate via a different mechanism due to the presence of small ions within their active layers. Pei and others proposed an electrochemical device mechanism reaching a p–i–n structure, in which if a large applied voltage is applied, an electrochemical doping takes place at the electrode interfaces, leading to the formation of conductive p- and n-doped regions flanking an insulating neutral region (i);45,69,70Fig. 5. While the ions stabilize the growing of the doped regions, the electron–hole recombination occurs in the neutral region. In 2005, Leger and co-workers noted that either p- or n-doped regions can be formed depending on the applied bias and the injection energy barriers.71,72 This was followed by the electrodynamic model proposed by deMello and Malliaras.73–75 This model predicts slow formation of electric double layers (EDLs) at the electrodes due to the drift of the mobile ions, leading to a strong interfacial electric field that assists charge injection. In 2010, Ginger and Kemerink explain that this model depends on the nature of the carrier injection and/or the formation of ohmic contacts.76,77 To summarize, poor carrier injection leads to the electrodynamic model where there is poor electrochemical doping due to the high injection barrier, but it is continuously enhanced over time due to the EDL formation. Thus, both or only one of the doped fronts are rapidly formed if the ohmic contacts are achieved at the applied bias, leading to an electrochemical regime.
 Fig. 5  Schematic representations of typical (I) LED, (II) OLED, and (III) LEC devices. 
1.3 Commercial promise of OLEDs and LECs
The quest for next-generation LEDs based on newly engineered molecules that work more efficiently and are less costly than their semiconductor counterparts, led to the realization of organic light emitting materials or OLEDs.78–81 Instead of rigid semiconductor materials, the emissive layer of OLEDs is comprised of polymers or small molecules that can be deposited directly from solution or imbedded into a polymer matrix onto a range of substrates such as glass, flexible soft plastics, ceramics, metals and clothing.26 Consequently, OLEDs can be molded into a particular shape or design, permitting the fabrication of a broad range of flexible, lightweight devices. Although OLED technology currently suffers from high manufacturing costs, cheaper forms of OLED lighting and the development of flexible OLED based form factors for hardware design are on the horizon. Furthermore, the flexibility of OLED lighting panels will provide new opportunities for the installation of designer lighting, where aesthetics is the primary consideration. Scientists are currently working on embedding this technology into car windshields, store windows, and mirrors (http://OLED-Info.com). Furthermore, companies are now designing smart clothes with chips that can be connected to OLED-based processors. In this way walls, glasses, and mirrors can advertise products that users have ‘liked’ on their Facebook pages or added to their Amazon ‘wish lists’. Samsung commercialized the world's first 55 inch OLED smart television in 2012. The television was recognized as a “Best of Innovation Awards Honoree” at the 2012 Consumer Electronics Trade Event (CES) for its fast response time, ability to view two channels on the same screen and three-dimensional (3D) glasses with appended ear phones.82 In the future, it is very likely that both residential and commercial buildings will be decorated with OLED ceiling and/or wall panels. Fig. 6 shows a windowless plane covered with OLED panels.
 Fig. 6  A windowless aeroplane fabricated by OLEDs which could be a reality in under a decade. Reproduced from ref. 83 with permission from the http://Independent.Co.Uk, copyright 2014. 
An article published in the UK's independent newspaper in 2014 displays the concept of a windowless plane. It states: “those minuscule windows on airplanes could soon become a thing of the past, with a UK developer working on windowless fuselages that house giant, flexible OLED screens”.83
As previously mentioned, the fabrication of OLEDs is currently expensive84,85 primarily due to the multilayer evaporation process, and the need for technological encapsulation which is necessary to preserve opto-electronic properties86,87 and lifetimes.88,89 Nevertheless, when compared to semiconductor LEDs, the direct correlation between molecular structure and emission wavelength permits fine tuning of the optical properties that in turn offers a broad range of color options, including the red, blue, green and white needed for display applications.67 A comprehensive summary of these factors can be found in Section 1.4 of this review.
Over the past two decades, LECs have emerged as attractive prospects for next-generation SSL.90–93 As explained above, the simplicity of their device structures and fabrication makes them very attractive for low-cost SSL technology. However, LECs perform typically less well than OLEDs with respect to lifetime, luminance, and efficiency, see Tables 2–11. This lower performance is attributed to the electrochemical degradation of the emitter, the lack of control of the position of the emissive neutral layer, as well as phase separation between the emitter and the electrolyte over time, under both storage and working conditions. However, the best performing LECs possess record efficiencies of 99.2 cd A−1 at a luminance of ca. 2000 cd m−2 together with stabilities of thousands of hours.94–96 This is only noted for yellow and orange LECs using the archetypal emitters that include conjugated polymers and Ir(III) complexes. However, this excellent performance must be also achieved in blue, orange and red LECs, regardless of the type of emissive material employed (small molecule, sustainable TMCs, QDs, etc.).

The main advantage that has fueled commercial interest in LECs is the facile fabrication of the devices using any 3D conductive substrates and very cheap solvent-based fabrication techniques such as roll-to-roll and spray-coating, Fig. 7.12,97–100 Here, it is important to note that changes in the nature of the electrolyte strongly affect the quality of the active layer (rheological aspects for film forming, morphology, phase separation, etc.), but this has permitted their fabrication into a broad range of shapes and substrates that include fibers for lighting clothing, light-emitting paper as well as kitchen utensils such as forks. Therefore, while current industrial research on OLEDs is driven by their high efficiency, color quality, and high contrast, the niche for LECs involves lighting uses for labeling, signaling, decoration and medical applications. This is in part driven by the low-cost associated with the fabrication of LECs. Edman and co-workers recently investigated the financial costs associated with different scenarios for LEC mass production by varying fabrication techniques and environments.101 Surprisingly, they concluded that the achievement of high performing LECs prepared by high-volume roll-to-roll coating procedures costs 0.0036 € per lm, which is orders of magnitude less than OLEDs.101 When this low cost is combined together with additional advantages such as areal emission, low weight, low working voltage, and tolerance to ambient working conditions it seems very likely that LEC technology will find its way into our daily life within the next few years.64 To-date just a few companies have recognized this potential and started to develop LECs for commercial applications.
 Fig. 7  Examples of non-conventional LECs prepared via industry-relevant solution-based techniques. (a) Photograph of a working inkjet printed CP-based LEC with an interdigitated distance of 1 mm. (b) Photograph of a fresh (left) and 6 months old (right) slot die-coated LEC driven at V = 7 V. (c) Photographs of a roll-to-roll-coated large area iTMC-based LEC. (d) Contrast-enhanced photographs of gravure-printed films using different LEC ink formulations – i.e., different PMMA molecular weights and concentrations. (e) Photographs of spray-sintered LECs featuring a large-area device (left) and a kitchen fork as the electrode (right). (f) Photographs of two fiber-like CP-based LECs integrated into textiles. (g) Photographic image of the patterned emission from an 8 × 8 mm2 line-art bilayer LEC device. The ‘fish’ pattern was created by ‘drawing’ in the electrolyte. (h) Photograph of textile LECs upon deformation between two fingers. Reproduced from ref. 11 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2017. 
1.4 A bright future for OLED technology
Over the past decade, OLED technology has been integrated into lighting devices which offer high resolution and bright energy-saving displays that do not need a backlight. Consequently, OLED devices are much thinner and lighter than their LCD or plasma counterparts and as such, are beginning to be widely implemented into the screens of portable devices and smart televisions.82 Recent advances in OLED applications are reflected in the substantial increase in revenue reported by Samsung Electronics, i.e., 52 billion US dollars in the second quarter of 2018, which is substantially larger than the 13.3 billion dollars reported for the first quarter of 2017.102 More than two-thirds of current TV screens larger than 40 inches contain OLED technology. For this application, there is currently competition between OLEDs and the rival quantum dot technology (QD-LEDs), which is also being pursued by the leading electronic companies. Currently, the higher price of QD-LEDs compared to OLEDs, is not compensated by the small gain in their overall performance and thus a financial barrier remains towards the broad scale applications of this competing technology. Academic researchers and corporate laboratories worldwide are extensively focused towards finding new market-place opportunities for OLED devices. In this respect, the leading manufacturer of OLED displays is currently Samsung, which uses this technology in its Galaxy smartphones. Although, OLED televisions represent the largest applications for these materials to-date, mobile phones are likely to supersede this. The future growth of OLEDs will provide new opportunities for chemists to supply new families of compounds to the optoelectronics industry that could potentially involve research budgets of billions of dollars, Fig. 8.
 Fig. 8  The projected growth for the OLED display market from 2016 to 2026. Mobile phone and TV data available only for 2016. Source: IDTechEx. Reproduced from ref. 103 with permission from the http://Cen.Acs.Org, copyright 2016. 
The company IDTechEx forecasts that the global sales of OLED displays will reach 42 billion US dollars in 2020. Guillaume Chansin, senior technology analyst at IDTechEx, stated “OLED displays can be lighter, they can be flexible, and they allow designers more scope with respect to engineering the shape of their devices (Fig. 9).” Despite these advantages, rival technology such as liquid-crystal displays (LCDs) still retains a significant portion of the market. Current LCD technology is based on the generation of backlight that is sent light through liquid crystals, polarizers, color filters, and several image-enhancing filters. OLED technology requires no such backlight as the image is created from independent red, blue and green sub-pixels.
 Fig. 9  The main commercial achievements of OLEDs from 2013 to March 2018. Source, OLED-Info Company. 
From an operational perspective, developing suitable blue OLEDs is challenging since fewer organic compounds and small molecules demonstrate blue emission. Furthermore, the chemical bonds in blue emitters are typically more sensitive to cleavage since OLEDs require more power to operate. The recombination process of the charge carriers mediated through the absorption of UV light is also more likely in blue compounds. For all electronic devices, the cost of the individual components is an important consideration. A large portion of OLEDs comprising of metal complexes contain iridium, which despite being amongst the earliest emissive materials affords displays that are expensive to manufacture at nearly $19 per g.
The next challenge concerns the vacuum deposition of the organic compounds and electrodes that typically requires harsh conditions i.e., 10−6 atm of pressure. During this process, in addition to the desired substrate, the emissive materials are also coated onto a mask which wastes between 70–90% of the compounds that must be subsequently removed. However, since only 1 g of this material is required for the fabrication of 3000 displays, this cost is unlikely to significantly affect the future growth of the OLED display market. As previously mentioned, almost all of the large electronic companies have heavily invested in OLED technology, including LG who invested $9 billion in November 2016 for the construction of an OLED TV plant. Compared to the future manufacturing of OLEDs, ink-jet printing should decrease fabrication costs and improve the overall performance of the displays, (Fig. 10). To date, DuPont's ink-jet inks are affording blue OLEDs that can perform at 95% after 2000 hours of continuous use.103 Despite these advances, the rate of progress of OLEDs for use in mobile phones has been much faster than TV displays. Since OLED displays are thinner, lighter, more flexible and robust than their LCD counterparts, it is generally believed that if their fabrication costs can be reduced then the whole display industry will ultimately shift to OLED technology in the near future. From an academic perspective, there has been an exponential growth of interest in the field of OLEDs in recent years as highlighted in Fig. 11. In fact, in a recent literature search, a total of 27229 articles including 1207 reviews were found with the title of “Organic Light Emitting Diode”. This highlights the global importance of this research field that includes significant contributions both from academia and industry. Several reviews highlighting the most important concepts relating to OLED and LEC performance have been published in recent years.104–107 There are also valuable reviews concerning the application of organometallic complexes in optoelectronic devices that include solar cell, OLED, water splinting and hydrogen production.108 Moreover, there have been several comprehensive reviews published on solid state LEDs based on ionic transition metal complexes (iTMCs) including Ir(III) complexes,11,109–111 however none have correlated the influence of the ancillary ligand in the transition metal emitter with the electroluminescence performance of the resulting complex in a solid state LED. In fact, review articles to-date discuss the main factors concerning device performance, modification of the components within each layer of the device, as well as device fabrication and encapsulation methodologies.112,113 In this review, we focus on the discovery, optimization and importance of TADF in polypyridyl transition metal complexes. The motivation behind this review stems from the urgent need for the rational design of TADF emitters to enhance their efficiencies and decrease processing costs for commercial applications.
 Fig. 10  Ink-jet printers, such as this one developed by Kateeva, may ultimately lower the future fabrication costs of OLED displays. Reproduced from ref. 103 with permission from the http://Cen.Acs.Org, copyright 2016. 
 Fig. 11  Number of publications annually of OLEDs (data collected from Web of Science, Feb 19, 2019). 
1.5 Selection criteria for organometallic emitters
Among all of the emitters reported to-date, cyclometalate complexes have attracted considerable interest for their novel photophysical properties that are well suited to a broad range of applications.114–118 Since the first discovery of highly efficient OLEDs prepared from cyclometalate Ir(III) complexes, hundreds of organometallic compounds have now been reported.119,120 Cyclometalate complexes have the general formula [M(X)3−n(Y)n]x+ and are comprised of a metal cation (Mx+), a bidentate chelating ligand involved in at least one organometallic carbon-to-metal bond, as well as an ancillary ligand, see Fig. 12.
 Fig. 12  Three generations of OLEDs. The first organic and inorganic materials with high EQEs. The EQE values of I, II and III are taken from ref. 121, 122 and 123, respectively. 
The metal center is generally a cation with a full tn2g configuration e.g. Ir(III), Ru(II), Os(II), or Pt(II). In these complexes, the redox properties of the metal cations facilitate electron transport between the layers of the device. An Ir(III) complex comprising of the benchmark ligand bis[2-(4,6-fluorophenyl)pyridinato-C2,N] is shown in Fig. 13. The ancillary ligand (Y) can be mono- or multidentate, that is typically chosen to modulate the HOMO of complex.124,125 One major advantage of this family of compounds lies in their emissive properties that span a broad range of wavelengths. This makes them particularly well-suited as the emissive components of SSL point sources and display applications.126 The key role of the ancillary ligand is to tune the opto-electronic properties of the TMC, which is also important for dye-sensitized solar cell (DSSC) applications.127 In this respect, the up-conversion of photoluminescent compounds, which are able to produce near infrared emission is also promising for the development of light-emitting devices for solar cell applications.128–137 Furthermore, studies over the past few decades have revealed that it is the ancillary ligands of cyclometalate complexes that influence the key properties of the resulting OLED devices, which include luminescence intensity, stability, time to achieve maximum emission (Tmax), maximum emission wavelength or color, and tuning of the energy band gap. In this context, ligand substitution reactions to replace the aforementioned N,N′ ancillary ligands by N,N′, N,O, and O,O′ bidentate donors have been carried out to achieve better matches between the emissive and hole/electron transport properties. Tuning the structural and physical properties of the ligands by extending the π-conjugation and introducing electron donating/accepting groups have also been reported to modulate the color of the light emission and improve OLED performance.138–141 A more in depth discussion of this strategy applied to different families of emissive complexes is presented in Sections 2–6 of this review.
 Fig. 13  Chemical drawing and ORTEP plot of the molecular structure of FIrpic. Reproduced from ref. 142 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. 
The archetypal emitter for electroluminescence devices is bis[2-(4,6-difluorophenyl)pyridinato-C2,N](picolinato)Ir(III) (Fig. 8), more commonly referred to as FIrpic or Ir(diFppy)2(pic). This complex is the most well studied cyclometalate Ir complex reported to-date and is widely used as a blue phosphorescent material. FIrpic is ideal for OLED applications since in addition to its suitable photophysical and electrochemical properties, it is straightforward to synthesize and it is stable and easy to structurally modify.142
1.6 Challenges and limitations in the market-place
Concerning the discussion regarding the commercialization and global marketplace opportunities for OLED and LEC devices, appropriate parameters (i.e., figures of merit – FoM) to compare the emission efficiencies of different compounds are necessary. In this respect, two of most the useful parameters to evaluate the emission efficiency are luminance (cd m−2), which conveys the amount of light emitted from a given area of a device and radiant power or flux (lm), which is defined as the radiant energy emitted. Luminance divided by the current density (A m−2) gives the current efficiency (cd A−1). It is important to note that device efficiency cannot be evaluated directly from the photometric parameters because it is not possible to directly convert cd and lm into physical units. In order to overcome this problem, EQE or ηφ, and power efficiency (PE) are more commonly used to relay device physics. In this respect, ηφ is related to the number of photons emitted from the surface of the device per number of injected charges and provides a measurement of the overall efficiency of the device, which is typically reported as a percentage. In contrast, PE defines the total radiation power from a unit area of an emission surface per second divided by the electric power density supplied to the device and is reported in units of lm W−1. In terms of efficiency, OLEDs can compete with highly efficient conventional light sources, but their efficiencies typically diminish at high brightness levels, an effect known as efficiency roll-off. The origin of efficiency roll-off is generally attributed to a decline in the charge-carrier balance and non-radiative quenching. This is particularly critical in LECs, in which parameters such as microcavity effects, self-absorption from the doped regions and electrode quenching must be taken into account in designing both the emitter and the device architecture. Therefore, LECs are still less competitive in terms of efficiency when directly compared to OLEDs and LEDs. It should also be noted that all the aforementioned parameters are routinely used to evaluate device efficiency. In this review, we will therefore use these FoM to highlight the performance of devices and assess the progress of device efficiency as a function of molecular design.143
In the next section, we will discuss the major factors that govern the emission efficiencies including: (1) the quantum efficiency of luminescence; (2) charge carrier balance factor; (3) out-coupling factor and (4) the lowering of drive voltage. It is important to note, that the main problem concerning the quantum efficiencies of the luminescence in these compounds was initially based on the assumption that the ratio of singlet to triplet excited states produced by carrier recombination could not theoretically exceed 25%. This number is attributed to the upper limit imposed on the system by the 3:1 statistics of the electron spin states formed under electrical excitation, since light can only be produced from singlet excitons. This means that if the recombination process is independent of spin, a limitation will always be encountered when compared to the luminescence efficiency of OLEDs, which is defined by the fact that the internal quantum efficiency (ηint) cannot exceed 25%. In practice, however, this limitation has now been overcome, for example Ir coordination complexes have been reported with ηint = 100% and non Ir-based systems are also known with ηint values close to 100%. In these systems, singlet excited states are generated through triplet–triplet annihilation (TTA), or a process more commonly referred to as TADF.144–149
Charge balance factors are more relevant to third generation emitters that significantly influence the device efficiency.150 However, more experimental and theoretical studies are required to acquire a better understanding of these factors. Recently, theoretical studies of OLEDs have shed light on the basic concept of carrier balance relating to the electron and hole recombination efficiency within the organic layer of the device.151 It is also important however to establish if carrier recombination zones are also present throughout the multiple layers of the device, including the doped regions in the LECs.
The light out-coupling factor χ is defined by eqn (1): (1)For refractive indices, n > 1.6 the right-hand side of the equation simplifies to (2n2)−1. For organic compounds with refractive indices close to 1.6, the value of χ is around 0.20. This conveys the fact that ∼50% of the phonons in the device are lost due to total internal reflection (TIR) and a further 30% are lost through the glass substrate and/or the ITO/organic layers, i.e., they are either trapped inside the substrate, or emitted out from the edges of the device. In LECs, we must also take into account other mechanisms for phonon loss involving self-absorption of the doped regions and changes of the position of the neutral emitting layer over time.152–154 One of the most important challenges is therefore to develop devices with light extraction capabilities χ > 20%, which clearly exceed the limitations associated with low-refractive indices.155 Numerous efforts have been made to improve the external coupling efficiencies via improved device concepts. The latter include modifications to the electrodes, syntheses of new organic materials and device fabrication. However, the added complexity of light extraction due to its relationship with other device characteristics makes this parameter difficult and challenging to optimize.156–158 The final factor to consider concerning the optimization of device performance is the applied voltage required to switch on the device. Here, we must bear in mind, that in contrast to the electroluminescence quantum efficiency, the power conversion efficiency is inversely proportional to the applied voltage (V). To decrease the applied voltage, several methods such as the introduction of buffer layers to stabilize the interface between the electrodes and the emissive organic layers have been proposed. Applying these concepts, the lowest turn on voltage reported to-date is 2.3 V for [Ru(bpy)3](ClO4)2.159 which can be further lowered (<2 V) using redox co-reactants in the active layer.160
Today, at least in principle scientist know how to design devices with ηint approaching 100%. However, realistically this value is not >40% in a real device due to the aforementioned limitations. For high performing lighting applications, devices with high luminances of 5000 cd m−2 are required. Thus, a further reduction in applied voltage under high current density is still needed to achieve the levels of power conversion efficiency necessary to reach these goals. However, the most serious problem for broad scale applications of OLED and LECs is the long-term decrease in luminance after continuous operation of a constant current. As shown in Fig. 14, the luminance of OLEDs in a constant-electric current with a driving voltage gradually decreases on a logarithmic time scale reaching 104 h. In the case of LECs, the record stability is now around thousands of hours using constant and pulsed driving modes.57,95 It is therefore crucial to find a solution to this problem in order to prolong the lifetimes of both OLED and LEC devices to the greater than 10000 hours necessary for practical applications. In addition to understanding the chemical degradation of the molecular components of the devices after long term electrical excitation,161,162 future efforts to understand the resulting photo-chemical and photo-physical effects are also needed.
 Fig. 14  (a) Roll-off in EQE under high-current-density drive conditions; (b) long-term decay in EQE in constant-current-density mode. Multiple lines indicate that the roll-off or efficiency decay originates from multiple unknown parameters. Reproduced from ref. 143 with permission from the Japan Society of Applied Physics, copyright 2013. 
1.7 Third generation emitters based on the thermally activated delayed fluorescence (TADF)
As previously mentioned, device efficiency is strongly dependent on (i) the capability of the materials to convert injected charges into photons and (ii) the light out-coupling. In this respect, the TADF emission process is a very attractive mechanism to optimize the performances of devices, since both the produced and lost triplet excitons of fluorescence are converted into emissive singlets. The importance of this phenomenon is reflected in the dramatic increase in publications related to TADF-OLEDs over the past 5 years, which have had significant impact in both the photonic and optics research communities, Fig. 15. This has, however, had a lower impact on the LEC world, since the efficiencies and stabilities of devices comprising of TADF emitters have not yet reached high enough values for practical applications.163 In fact, just a few studies have demonstrated TADF electroluminescence behavior in Cu(I) complexes50,52,53,164,165 and small molecules.60,166 Therefore, a general discussion in the next section of this paper is focused on the development of TADF emitters for OLEDs. It is important to note that TADF molecules can now overcome the previously mentioned intrinsic 25% limitation imposed on fluorescence materials, achieving maximum ηint values of 100%, Fig. 16.167,168
 Fig. 15  Number of publications annually (a), total publication by year (b) sum of times cited per item which dealing TADF showing the near exponential growth in interest in recent years (data collected from Web of Science September 15, 2018). 
 Fig. 16  Schematic representation of transitions with absorption and rate constants k. r and nr refer to radiative and non-radiative processes, and S and T refer to the transition from S1 and T1 states, respectively. Reproduced from ref. 168 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2013. 
The crucial factor to consider when designing a TADF OLED is the energy gap, ΔEST, between the lowest singlet (S1) and triplet (T1) excited states. When ΔEST is small, reverse intersystem crossing (RISC) from T1 can result in TADF (Fig. 16 and 17). In this respect, the radiative transition to the ground state (S0) via the thermal activation of molecules, results in TADF to an S1 excited state.169 There is therefore competition between TADF and phosphorescence processes through RISC and ISC, respectively (Fig. 17). It should also be noted that the electroluminescence efficiency of an OLED also increases as ΔEST decreases. For example, benzophenone derivatives are ketone based materials with relatively small values of ΔEST (0.1–0.2 eV) that exhibit TADF, but no intense luminescence at room temperature.170 In benzophenone, there is a small overlap between the non-bonded n-orbital on the oxygen and the excited π-orbital of the aromatic group. This small overlap leads to a small ΔEST that favors TADF. Furthermore, since their initial discovery by Adachi et al.,147 these compounds are now recognized as “third generation” OLEDs. In contrast, complexes comprising of condensed polycyclic aromatic ligands, such as anthracene, have ΔEST's greater than 1 eV and only display intense phosphorescence. From a quantum chemistry perspective, ΔEST reflects the overlap in the wave functions between the ground and excited states of the molecule, hence a subsequent decrease in the value of ΔEST leads to a decrease in this overlap (Fig. 17). In anthracene there is a strong overlap between the π and π* orbitals which affords a larger, less favorable ΔEST. Unfortunately, this situation is further complicated by the fact that a relatively large radiative rate constant (kr) is also required to design an efficient OLED, and a small ΔEST is not necessarily conducive to a large kr. An important challenge in this field is therefore the discovery of new molecules with small ΔEST and large kr. A practical approach to address this issue is to chemically tune the electron donating and withdrawing groups of the molecules to decrease the spatial overlap between their respective HOMO and LUMO frontier orbitals. Following this strategy, many families of small organic molecules, including triazines,171 spiro derivatives,172 spiro-acridine,145 phenoazine and carbazolyl dicyanobenzenes (CDCB)147 have been synthesized and studied over recent years. For an informative overview of this family of compounds the reader is directed to the review of Bryce et al.173 In addition to organic based TADF-OLEDs, inorganic systems have also attracted interest and noble metal-free TADF-OLEDs have also been reported.174
 Fig. 17  Mechanisms for the generation of singlet and triplet excitons upon electrical excitation. (a) Traditional fluorescence and phosphorescence; (b) for TADF compounds with S1 and T1 levels that are close in energy, reverse energy transfer is very efficient, facilitating EL from the S1 level. Reproduced from ref. 167 with permission from the Japan Society of Applied Physics, copyright 2014. 
1.8 Why inorganic molecule-based TADFs?
At first glance, research progress in the field of organic-TADFs seems to have progressed very quickly. However, in recent years, research concerning their inorganic counterparts has rapidly gained momentum since they offer several important advantages that include: (1) enhanced stabilities due to the presence of metal–ligand bonds; (2) higher spin–orbit coupling (SOC) when compared to their organic counterparts that increases intersystem crossing (ISC)175 and (3) access to a broad range of organic ligands, permitting the optimization of emission color, band gap and the reduction of non-radiative deactivation processes.176 In this respect, incorporating sterically hindered ligands reduces geometrical distortions in the triplet excited states which enhances their overall quantum yields.177,178 Another approach to enhance their properties is to introduce small functional groups to improve the hole/electron injection properties of the emissive layers.179,180 To summarize, there is more scope to functionalize inorganic-TADFs when compared to their organic counterparts, since varying the organic ligands and the coordination number and geometries of the metal cations provides complementary strategies for chemically tuning their opto-electronic properties. Although, the rapid growth of TADF-OLEDs has been rather unexpected, several issues still need to be addressed before these materials realize their true commercial potential. In this respect, the most important problem is their long-term stability. Studies focused towards understanding degradation mechanisms present in TADF-OLEDs report a half-lives of ca. 2800 h when operated at an initial luminance of 1000 cd m−2,181,182 confirming that more stability is needed before these devices can be reliably utilized as the components of lighting sources and displays. Research efforts by Adachi et al. have significantly increased our fundamental understanding of the degradation processes in OLED devices. Adachi's work reveals that the host molecules can play a key role in contributing to the instability of an OLED cell during photo irradiation via the formation of active radicals and unstable species (Fig. 18).183 Stabilization of the excited triplet states of the light emitting molecules upon irradiation is therefore one of the major challenges at the forefront of this field which still needs to be resolved.184
 Fig. 18  Changes in I/I0 of OLEDs, 15 wt% (4s,6s)-2,4,5,6-tetra-(9H-carbazol-9-yl)isophthalonitrile (4CzIPN):3,3-di(9H-carbazol-9-yl)biphenyl (mCBP) composite films, EODs, and HODs as a function of the operational time. Reproduced from ref. 183 with permission from the Japan Society of Applied Physics, copyright 2013. 
2 Second generation emitters based on cyclometalate Ir(III) complexes
2.1 Ir(III) complexes containing 2-phenylpyridine (ppy) and 2,2′-bpyridine/1,10-phenanthroline ancillary ligands
The first reported single-component emitter was a Ru(II) complex that has been successfully utilized to fabricate thin-film electroluminescence devices, using a variety of simple-solution processing techniques.124 Electroluminescent devices fabricated from this complex have shown high electroluminescence efficiencies and luminance levels of 100 cd m−2. The first LEC containing Ir(III), [Ir(ppy)2(dtbbpy)](PF6) (1) (where ppy = 2-phenylpyridine and dtbbpy = 4,4′-di-tert-butyl-2,2′-dipyridyl), was specifically designed by Slinker et al. for use as a multifunctional chromophore for a single-layer electroluminescent device.47 In this complex, two ppy ligands are coordinated to an Ir(III) cation which further increases the ligand field stabilization energy. One advantage of devices containing this complex are their high luminous power efficiencies which exceed 10 lm W−1 at −3 V.47 Unfortunately, the degradation of these devices has also been reported, that is attributed to the current-induced heating of the organic layer, resulting in the decomposition of the dtbbpy ligand.185 Later, the same group demonstrated that an improvement in the turn-on time for the onset of light emission, Ton for electroluminescent devices based on complex 1 could be achieved by introducing the ionic liquid (IL), 1-butyl-3-methyl imidazolium hexafluorophosphate (BMIP) into the electron injection layer.186 The effect of the IL on the Ton is quite significant, in which a blend of 0.46 mol of the IL per mol of complex 1 dramatically decreases the Ton from just 5 to 40 min. According to Fig. 19, Ton and Tmax decrease exponentially with respect to the concentration of the IL.186 Highly efficient phosphorescence along with a shift in the emissive properties from yellow to blue was achieved via the introduction of dimethylamino substituents in the 4 and 4′-positions of the bipyridine ligand in complex 2 (N926).187 In this respect, the introduction of the dimethylamino group destabilizes the LUMO orbitals of the bpy ligand that subsequently increases the energy gap between the HOMO and LUMO of complex N926, when compared to its unsubstituted counterpart complex 1.
 Fig. 19  Turn-on times Tmax (circles) and Ton (triangles) versus the concentration of ionic liquid for ITO/1:BMIP/Au devices under a −3 V bias. Reproduced from ref. 186 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2005. 
Surprisingly, a very small turn on voltage of 2.5 V was also reported for an LEC containing this complex.187 Moreover, the development of new ionic ligands has been a useful strategy to decrease the Tmax of solid state LEDs that afforded the next family of heteroleptic Ir complexes, 3–5.188 Related studies to introduce an alkyl appended NEt3+ substituent onto the 5 position of the bipyridine ligand reveal that extending the chain length of the CH2 linker from n = 1 to 4, demonstrates a significant enhancement in the efficiency of 5. In contrast, chain lengths of n > 5, reduce the efficiency of the device due to the unfavorable distances between the phosphorescent metal centers, which hinders electron transport. Efficient white light emission has been achieved for the Ir based system 6, by rationally combining red, blue and green emitters together within a single host–guest system.189 In this respect, in order to obtain white emission, a low concentration of the red-emitting guest (6) was doped into the blue-green-emitting host [Ir(dfppz)2(dedaf)](PF6), (where dfppz = 1-(2,4-difluorophenyl)pyrazole and dedaf = 9,9-diethyl-4,5-diazafluorene) to induce partial energy transfer. The resulting device displays a white EL spectrum with Commission Internationale de l’Eclairage (CIE) coordinates ranging from (x, y) = (0.45, 0.40) to (0.35, 0.39), between 2.9 and 3.3 V, and high color-rendering indices (CRI) of up to 80, which is a critical measure for solid-state lighting.
One creative approach to improve the stabilities of LECs has been to employ the supramolecular caged iTMC, 7.190 This novel complex afforded bright (2700 cd m−2 at 5 V), very stable LECs with an estimated t1/2 of approximately 600 h at 3 V, where t1/2 is defined as the time to half maximum brightness. Inter- and intramolecular π–π interactions stabilize the ground and excited states of the complex, resulting in LEC's with substantially larger lifetimes τ of 3000 h at 3 V and 200 cd m−2, without sacrificing device Tmax.191 These values represent an enormous improvement when compared to the record lifetime of 30 hours for [Ir(ppy)2(bpy)] (PF6) LECs. Interestingly, the presence of a pendant phenyl substituent on the 6-position of the bpy ligand results in a cage effect that restricts the opening of the molecular structure of complex 8 in the excited 3MC state, thereby reducing the possibility of degradation reactions. Subsequent studies on the disubstituted complex 9 reveal stronger intra- and intermolecular π–π interactions that are detrimental to the overall stability of the device, which, as a consequence, has a lifetime only 1300 h at 3 V and 70 cd m−2.192 These low performances are linked with the loss of the planarity of the bpy ligand when the phenyl substituents π-stack with the phenyl groups of the ppy ligands, that in turn makes the complex more likely to undergo emission loss and degradation. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations support this explanation revealing that the energy difference between the emitting triplet and the metal-centered triplet (3MC) states in the complex are decreased, thus rendering it more susceptible to emission losses and degradation reactions. The introduction of bulky substituents into the 5,5′-positions of the 2,2′-bipy ligand were carried out to improve the stability of the devices by spacing out the molecules of the emissive layer in order to reduce chromophore–chromophore self-quenching processes. Sun et al. have shown that a device lifetime of 100 h at a bias of −4 V can be obtained using complex 10 with phenyl substituents on the 5,5′-positions of its ancillary bpy ligands.193
Over the years, it has become apparent that preventing the quenching of the excitons in solid-state LEDs results in more durable devices. This can be achieved via the introduction of bulky substituents that weaken π–π stacking interactions, which, in turn, increases the distance between neighboring metal complexes that helps to suppress the quenching. Following this strategy, three Ir(III) complexes 11–13 were designed bearing a phenyl substituent at the 6- and a bulky substituent at the 4-positions of the 2,2′-bpyridine auxiliary ligand.194 It has been demonstrated that the photoluminescence quantum yields (PLQYs) of complexes 11–13 increase both in solution and in thin films, as the size of the substituent on the 4-position of the bpy ligand increases.191 In this way, the introduction of large electronically inactive groups affords considerably shorter Tmax, higher luminances, higher efficiencies and longer device stabilities. Furthermore, the addition of a small amount of BMIP enhances the dynamic configuration of iTMC-based LECs, resulting in considerably enhanced stabilities of several hundreds of hours after driving the LECs with a pulsed current.195 This led to a sub-second light turn-on, exceptionally stable luminances >650 cd m−2 and power efficiencies with lifetimes >4000 h.
Following these studies, Schneider et al. prepared and studied the bis-cyclometalated Ir(III) complex 14 with a 2-naphthyl substituent at the 6-position of the ancillary 2,2′-bpyridine ligand.196 This ligand was chosen for two reasons: (i) the configuration of the excited state of the complex should be stabilized via π–π interactions and (ii) it contains bulky substituents at the 6-position of the ancillary ligand that should serve to reduce intermolecular interactions that subsequently decrease self-quenching. Complex 14 displays an emission maximum between 595–600 nm both in solution and in the thin film, consistent with emission from a triplet state with a large amount of charge-transfer character. Devices based on complex 14 have shown luminances, exceeding 300 cd m−2 and exceptional stabilities of more than 350 h.
The insertion of dinonyl substituents into the 4,4′-positions of the bpy ligand was pursued to prevent the self-quenching of blue or yellow solid state LEDs based on the Ir(III) complex 15.197 The resulting LEC device displayed a maximal brightness of 549 cd m−2 at 15.3 V and a ηext of 0.17 cd A−1. Furthermore, photobleaching of the luminescence intensity was observed after exposure to light under ambient conditions, attributed to the photoreaction of the complex with oxygen or water (Fig. 20). For complex 15, the color of the electroluminescence was tuned from blue to yellow via the addition of PVK host. Following this study, 4,4′-dinonyl-2,2′-pyridine was utilized as the ancillary ligand to prepare the Ir(III) soft salt (16), which was subsequently combined with an anionic Ir complex containing thiocyanate ligands.198 LECs prepared from these complexes revealed that the variation in color could be attributed to temperature dependent changes in the intermolecular interactions between the molecules in the emissive layers. High performances were obtained from electroluminescent phosphorescent OLEDs (PhOLEDs) fabricated by spin-coating techniques, demonstrating that soft salt-based PhOLEDs could be promising alternatives to conventional PhOLEDs fabricated from neutral complexes.
 Fig. 20  Photobleaching observed for the emissive layer of an LEC in air under ambient conditions; (a) the film of PVK containing crystals of 15 and (b) the same film after 1 min of exposure under the microscope. Reproduced from ref. 197 with permission from the Elsevier, copyright 2011. 
One pathway for the degradation of Ir-iTMCs in LECs is thought to involve nucleophilic attack of the positively charged metal centers. There are two ways to decrease this undesirable process, firstly by enhancing the hydrophobicity of the iTMCs, and secondly by targeting metal complexes with bulky ligands, which sterically protect the metal center. Following this approach, the new series of Ir-iTMCs (17–20) were devised, with phenyl and methyl substituents at the 6- and 4-positions of the bpy ligand in the archetypal [Ir(ppy)2(bpy)](PF6) complex.199 According to DFT calculations, the introduction of these substituents does not cause significant changes to the coordination sphere of the Ir(III) cations, but alters their HOMO–LUMO energy gap, shifting the emissive properties of the complexes to the green, which is also consistent with their electroluminescence properties (Fig. 21). Among this last family of complexes, the best stability was observed for complex 20, whose bpy ligand is substituted at both the 4,4′- and 6-6′-positions.
 Fig. 21  Electroluminescence spectra of devices incorporating complexes 17–20 in an applied bias of 3 V. Reproduced from ref. 199 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2011. 
As part of conceptual studies describing the correlation between the chemical structure and photophysical properties of iTMCs, complexes, [Ir(ppy)2(bpy)](PF6) (17) and [Ir(ppy)2(phen)](PF6) (21) were reported by Costa et al. Complex 21 was then subsequently used as the active emitter in LECs.200 Adding small amounts of IL to the Ir-iTMC dramatically improved both the luminescence and stability of the corresponding LEC devices. As previously described, these improvements were attributed to the increase in distance between neighboring molecules in the thin films, which is thought to reduce unfavorable self-quenching effects, supported by DFT calculations. For the same reason, the use of bulky ligands also greatly enhances the lifetimes of Ru(II)-complex-based LECs. Studies have also shown that employment of the organometallic Ir complex 22, containing a bulky, 4,7-diphenyl-1,10-phenanthroline-bis(2-phenylpyridine) ligand dramatically increases the lifetimes of single-layer devices, when compared to devices prepared from less bulkier derivatives.201 As a consequence, new Ir complexes of general formula [Ir(ppy)2(phen)](PF6) (23–27) containing ligands substituted with bulky side chains were explored for applications in single-layer electroluminescent LECs.202,203 In these complexes, the phen ligands are substituted with 9,9′-dihexylfluorenyl (23 and 24) and carbazole (25 and 26) moieties. Dihexylfluorenyl substituents were also introduced to increase the hydrophobicity and solubility, as well as to extend the π-systems to optimize the processability, stability and electron/hole-transport properties of the resulting devices.202 For the bulkyer, extended conjugated ligands in complexes 24 and 26, the increase in separation between the molecules in the thin film suppresses quenching, leading to a reduction in exciton migration, which in turn significantly enhances the lifetimes of their excited states.202,203 Unfortunately, higher drive voltages are required for the bulkier complexes since their charge carrier mobilities are reduced, which in turn reduces their luminous power efficiencies. DFT calculations on these systems reveal that in the singlet ground-state, the HOMO is localized over the metal cation and the benzene rings of the phenylpyridine ligands, whereas the LUMO is predominantly localized on the phenanthroline ligand.202 A maximum brightness of 1000 cd m−2 was attained at a bias of 3 V for complex 24, further highlighting the beneficial effect of its bulky substituents.
To exploit the synergy between an ionic Ir(III) complex and a perylenediimide (PDI) fluorescent red emitter, the PDI–Ir dyad (28) was prepared, which is one of the best deep red-emitting complexes reported to date.204 In this system, the Ir(III) complex functions as both the hole conductor and phosphorescence emitter, while PDI is the electron conductor. A LEC based on this dyed system has a high external EL efficiency in the deep-red region due to the high quantum yield of its PL and the short lifetimes of the excitons. The Ir(III) complex 29, containing a 2-phenyl-phen ligand, capable of displaying intramolecular π–π stacking interactions in the solid state has also been reported.205 The phenyl substituent was introduced to sterically block nucleophilic attack of the metal cations, a strategy that reduces self-quenching and increases the stability of LEC devices. Interestingly, the π–π interactions in this complex also stabilize the formation of a supramolecular cage in both the ground and excited state. A comparison of LECs based on 29 with similar LECs fabricated from supramolecular caged bipyridine-based Ir(III) complexes revealed that in these systems, the metal–ligand bond lengths in the 3MC state are important, since they contribute to the overall stability of the devices. Ir(III) complexes containing 5-methyl-phen (30) and 5,6-dimethyl-phen (31) ancillary ligands were also prepared and their electrochemical properties studied.206 LECs based on both of these complexes display yellow emission, with a low Von (approximately 3 V) and a high luminance. The device based on complex 31 had the best performance, with a maximum luminance and current density double that of complex 30, which is further supported by the absorption and PL spectra of the two systems. Ancillary phen ligands have been used to synthesize the series of cationic heteroleptic Ir(III) complexes (32–34).207 The PL spectra of complexes 32 and 33 display peaks at λmax = 546 and 540 nm, while the λmax of complex 34 is significantly red-shifted by ∼60 nm. This shift is attributed to the presence of the phenyl substituents on the ortho positions of the phen ligand, which stabilizes the LUMO when compared to the alkyl-substituted derivatives in complexes 32 and 33. Moreover, the bulky nature of the phenyl substituents also improves the luminance, current density and stability of the resulting LECs. Further studies on complex 34 reveal that the phenyl groups also acts as a physical barrier to protect the negative charge injected into the cationic Ir complex. Additional modifications to these complexes were achieved via the introduction of bipolar carrier-transporting units in the form of oxadiazole-triphenylamine auxiliary ligands, which were subsequently employed as phosphorescent dopants in polymer light-emitting diodes (PLEDs).208 The cationic Ir(III) complexes 35–37 are all amorphous and display high thermal stabilities, good solubilities, and thus are suitable for applications as phosphorescent dopants in PLEDs with the configuration ITO/PEDOT:PSS/35–37:PVK:PBD/TPBI/CsF/Al.208 Their PL and EL spectra were very similar, although the complexes were modified further with carrier-transporting groups. Among these complexes, complex 35 had the highest efficiency, most likely due to its bipolar nature that is able to regulate the balance between hole and electron fluxes more efficiently.
2.2 Ir(III) complexes containing ppy ligands and other polypyridyl ancillary ligands
The syntheses and photophysical studies of a family of cyclometallated Ir(III) complexes with β-diketonate ligands were reported by Lamansky et al.209,210 For these complexes, the PLQYs are in the range 0.1–0.4%. By choosing the appropriate ligand, the emission of the Ir complexes could be tuned from 512 to 612 nm, resulting in color variations from green to red. OLEDs based on these complexes had high ηext, values ranging from 6 to 12.3%, with complex 38 displaying the highest efficiency (12.3%, 38 lm W−1, >50 cd A−1). The complex Ir(ppy)2(pq) (39), (where pq = 2-phenylquinoline), subsequently studied as an efficient phosphorescent material with dual-color emission suitable for applications in white OLEDs (WOLEDs).211 In order to tune the emission of these complexes towards the red, a new cyclometalated Ir(ppy)3 complex was reported bearing a formyl substituent on one of the ppy ligands (40).212 This complex displayed a strong orange emission at λmax = 600 nm. OLEDs containing this complex have shown the same characteristic emission in dichloromethane solution as when doped into a PVK host, implying that the energy transfer between the PVK and the Ir(III) complex is a very efficient process. Based on these results, chemical modification of only one of the organic ligands provided a useful strategy to tune the emission wavelength. A promising approach to improve the lifetime of OLEDs containing these complexes is to increase the glass transition temperature, which serves to suppress the crystallization of amorphous emitting materials, resulting in a greater stabilization of film-based devices. With this idea in mind, the novel Ir(III) complex 41, bearing a polymerizable functional group was synthesized and used as a dopant to fabricate phosphorescent EL devices.213 The EL devices prepared by doping complex 41 into a host material, such as BCP (2,9-dimethyl-4,7-diphenyl-1,10-phenanthroline), or poly(N-vinyl-carbazole) (PVCz), display a bright blue-green luminescence of ca. 12000 cd m−2. In addition, complex 41 was co-polymerized with VCz to form the Ir co-polymer 42. The EL device containing this copolymer in the light-emitting hole-transport layer had a higher EL intensity versus devices prepared via monomer-dispersed doping. Su et al. designed an ionic iridium complex with two imidazolium groups at the ends of the two alkyl side chains, [Ir(ppy)2(dC6MIM-daf)](PF6)3, where dC6MIM-daf = 9,9-bis[6-(3-methyl imidazolium)hexyl]-1-yl-4,5-diazafluorene (43), to investigate the effect of two imidazolium groups on the device efficiencies of LECs. The properties of complex 43 were compared to the reference complex [Ir(ppy)2(dC6-daf)](PF6) (44), where (dC6-daf = 9,9′-dihexyl-4,5-diazafluorene) with no imidazolium substituents.214 Similar electrochemical, photophysical properties and comparable EL efficiencies were reported for both complexes, but a significant decrease in turn-on times was recorded for LECs based on complex 43 when compared to complex 44. This advancement in turn-on times is attributed to the increase in density of mobile PF6− counterions, which in turn increase the ionic conductivity of the film containing ionic Ir complexes with imidazolium substituents. He et al. designed two blue-emitting cationic Ir complexes based on 2-(1H-pyrazol-1-yl)pyridine (pzpy) ancillary ligands as part of a strategy to shift the emission of Ir(III) complexes to the blue region of the visible spectrum.215 Complexes, [Ir(ppy)2(pzpy)](PF6) (45A) and [Ir(dfppy)2(pzpy)](PF6) (where dfppy = 2-(2,4-difluorophenyl)pyridine) (45B) both contain ancillary pzpy ligands that significantly destabilize the LUMO frontier orbitals, resulting in blue shifted emission. This shift is attributed to the electron-donating nature of the N atom of the pyrazole moiety, which increases the electron density on the pzpy ancillary ligand. [Ir(dfppy)2(pzpy)](PF6) has an anodically shifted oxidation potential and displays blue-shifted emission spectra when compared to [Ir(ppy)2(pzpy)](PF6) due to the presence of the electron-withdrawing fluorine substituents on its ppy ligand. LECs based on complexes 45A and 45B display green-blue electroluminescence with a relatively high efficiency of 4.3 cd A−1. Using complex 45B as the dopant, deep-blue electrophosphorescence was also obtained with a λmax at 458 nm and CIE coordinates of (0.16, 0.22).216 Modification of the dfppy ligand via the introduction of a pendant hydrophobic phenyl ring in complex 46, dramatically enhances the stability of the resulting LEC.217 As described earlier, the bulky nature of the phenyl substituent on the N atom prevents the complex from being readily attacked by nucleophilic molecules such as water.
