Hou et al. prepared and studied the properties of a number of Ir complexes and subsequently used them as the emissive components of highly efficient phosphorescent OLEDs.218–220 Studies on these systems reveal that amidinates are useful ancillary ligands in phosphorescent bis-cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes, affording significant improvements in the emissive properties of the complexes due to the reduction of self-quenching and decrease in sensitivity compared to the concentration of the dopant.218 As a consequence, complex 47 afforded low-driving-voltage red phosphorescent OLEDs over a broad range of doping concentrations, even when the complex was not doped directly into a host matrix. Substituting ppy for fluorine in 47 afforded a complex of formula (dfppy)2Ir(N,N′-diisopropylbenzamidine) that has superior hole and electron transport (HT and ET) properties, due to the well-matched energy levels between the HT and ET material.220 For this system, the high electron/hole mobility is attributed to the strong electron-withdrawing nature of its fluorine substituents, which lowers the energy levels of the LUMO and HOMO frontier orbitals when compared to 47. This novel complex was not only an efficient phosphorescent emitter, profiting from its own high PL efficiency in the solid state, but it is also an excellent host for a yellow phosphorescent dopant, affording high-performance PhOLEDs. Following these studies, guanidinate compounds were employed as ancillary ligands, and the novel family of structurally well-defined, phosphorescent heteroleptic cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes 48–58 were reported.221 Since guanidinate has an additional amino group on its central carbon when compared to amidate, it is more electron-donating, and thus an excellent choice of ancillary ligand for the stabilization of electron-deficient metal centers. This, in turn, serves to increase the hole injection (HI and HT) properties of the resulting complexes, which show intense emission at room temperature, with λmax values in the range of 528–560 nm and PLQYs of 0.16–0.37 (Fig. 22). As observed in Fig. 22, the electroluminescent (EL) emission of the complexes can be tuned from green to yellow by choosing the appropriate guanidinate ligand. The steric bulk and electron-donating capabilities of the guanidinate ligands protect the metal cations, prevent significant triplet–triplet annihilation and reduce non-emissive pathways facilitated via intermolecular excited-state interactions. In addition, these factors promote HI and hole transportation resulting in complexes with very favorable EL properties. In these systems, varying the ancillary ligand has only a small effect on the color of the emission. However, despite these observations, the relative energy levels of the main and ancillary ligands of the complexes permit their emissive properties to be tuned, providing a strategy to optimize the luminous efficiency of the devices by facilitating inter-ligand energy transfer (ILET).
 Fig. 22  EL spectra of complexes 48–58 (left) and (right) image of the fabricated devices at various driving voltages. Reproduced from ref. 221 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2011. 
In order to explore the modulation of the relative triplet states of the C,N and ancillary ligands, oxadiazole (OXD)-, benzoxazolate-, and benzothiazolate-based Ir(III) complexes (59–61) were synthesized and subsequently fabricated into OLEDs.222,223 The aforementioned ancillary ligands have high electron affinities, making them good candidates for electron injection and transport, as well as high PLQY and good chemical and thermal stabilities. Studies of these complexes revealed which of the EL properties were influenced by ILET.222,223 Expanding these studies further, hybrid fluorescent/phosphorescent white OLEDs, comprising of complex 61 that is a yellow/green phosphorescent emitter were fabricated.228 Subsequent studies reveal that a device prepared from complex 61 has a high color rending index (CRI) of up to 89, with EQE, CE and ηp of 20%, 55.2 cd A−1 and 49.6 lm W−1 respectively.228 Ligands based on 1,3,4-OXD were also used to synthesize a series of Ir(III) complexes that include 62 and 63 for subsequent applications in LECs.160 Studies show that systematically changing the substituents or replacing the OXD ligand varies the PL and EL properties of the device. Complexes 62 and 63 were reported to be red emitters with λmax = 616–624 nm (Fig. 23). LECs comprising of complexes 62 and 63 displayed very high efficiencies or brightness, which have been attributed to the lower amount of quenching of the MLCT transitions brought about by the OXD ancillary ligand.
 Fig. 23  Electroluminescence spectra of LECs of complexes 62 and 63. Reproduced from ref. 224 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2013. 
Ir(III) multilayer LECs comprising of complex 64 were also investigated.225 A layer of PVK was inserted beneath the Ir(III) layer to produce a pinhole-free device that facilitates hole transport due to the low solubility of the anionic Ir complex. This device emits a pale green light, which is almost independent of the driving voltage, showing stable CIE coordinates from 4 to 7 V. In addition to the adjustment of the wavelength of phosphorescence, the band widths of the electroluminescence in these complexes could also be tuned from blue to red via functionalization of their organic ligands with appropriate electron withdrawing or electron donating substituents.226 Following this strategy oxazoline (65) and thiazoline (66) containing heteroleptic Ir(III) complexes were prepared and doped into a CBP host to afford PhOLEDs with efficient green and yellow emission.226 Although the PhOLEDs prepared from these complexes had similar EL emission with λmax values between 530 and 540 nm, they had different colors due to the significant differences in the values of full width at half-maximum of their emission spectra, with 2 of them being 70 and 110 nm for complexes 65 and 66, respectively. DFT calculations on these systems reveal that the HOMO–LUMO energy gap for complex 65 is larger, presumably since the more polarizable S atom of the thiazoline increases the conjugation of the ligand, resulting in a smaller energy gap.
As previously mentioned, choosing suitable ancillary ligands to develop new complexes with blue-light emission is an important challenge at the forefront of this field. Blue-green-emitting Ir complexes have been designed and synthesized by introducing N,N′ chelates based on triazol-pyridine ligands (67–69).227 The N,N′-heteroaromatic ancillary ligands in these systems have high triplet energy levels, and the lone pair of electrons on their N atoms can effectively regulate the energy levels of their LUMOs. The order of the peak maxima in their emission spectra is Ir(ppy)3 > 67 > 68 > 69, which is correlated to the increase in the electron-withdrawing capability of their organic ligands. A device based on complex 69 was reported to have the best performance, displaying the highest current and power efficiency, while devices based on complexes 67 and 68 display lower roll-off efficiencies, attributed to the increase in conjugation of their organic ligands. Furthermore, the larger torsional angle between the triazole and phenyl rings in ligand 67 increases the steric hindrance, resulting in a lower roll-off efficiency when compared to devices containing complex 68.
2.3 Ir complexes containing ppy ligands functionalized with large aromatic substituents
The synthesis and characterization of a new family of phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes assembled from diphenylaminofluorene ligands (70 and 71) was carried out to explore the potential of Ir(III) phosphors for applications as emissive dopants in OLEDs that are deposited via vacuum deposition.229 Charged, emissive metal complexes with short lifetimes both solution and the solid state were successfully vacuum-deposited without any significant decomposition. Due to the higher PL quantum efficiencies of complex 70, the resulting OLED devices A–C were doped with three concentrations namely, 2.5% (A), 5% (B), and 8% (C). Examining Fig. 24, it is apparent that the brightness of the device at a given current density slightly increases as the concentration of the dopant is increased from 2.5 to 8 wt%. Additional studies revealed that devices doped with a 5 wt% of complex 70 yielded the most efficient electrophosphorescence with a brightness greater than 15000 cd m−2 and power efficiencies and luminance as high as 19 lm W−1 and 20 cd A−1, respectively.
 Fig. 24  (a) Current density vs. voltage and (b) luminance vs. voltage for OLED devices containing three doping concentrations of complex 70 in a CBP host. Reproduced from ref. 229 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2007. 
A new family of highly efficient red-emitting phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes (72–77), were prepared using carbazole (CVz)–phenylquinoline (PhQ) and picolinic acid N-oxide (pic-N-O) ligands, and subsequently used for the fabrication of PhOLEDs.230 CVz ligands are electron-donating and thus their complexes display relatively intense luminescence and excellent photoconductivities. In addition, PhQ ligands are well-known for their ability to afford conjugated systems displaying excellent mechanical properties together with electronic, opto-electronic, and/or non-linear optical (NLO) properties. Cyclic voltammetry studies of these complexes, Fig. 25, reveal that their electrochemical band gaps lie between 2.50 and 2.52 eV, which are consistent with their optical band gaps (2.44–2.50 eV), recorded at the onset of their absorption spectra. All six complexes possess high thermal stabilities and emit intense red light with excellent color purities and CIE coordinates (0.65, 0.34). A maximum brightness of 18500 cd m−2 was achieved at 215 mA cm−2 for a device containing complex 73 as the dopant. Complexes containing pic ancillary ligands displayed high current densities at the same voltage due to their high electron mobilities.230 In this respect, pic and pic-NO ligands have stronger negative inductive effects when compared to their acac counterparts. The performances of PhOLEDs fabricated from this family of Ir(III) complexes are in good agreement with results from DFT calculations. Following this work,230 a new bulky trimethylsilylxylene-substituted Ir(III) complex 78, containing a pic-ancillary ligand was synthesized in high yields via a Suzuki coupling reaction.231 PhOLEDs containing this new dopant exhibited yellow-green emission with a λmax = 528 nm. Since the ionic nature of the complex affects not only the Ton, but also the stability of the resulting devices, Kwon et al. reported a new method to reduce the Ton and increase stabilities of light-emitting LECs by using the neutral Ir complex 79.232 Complex 79 containing a pendant Na+ ion was introduced instead of the classic iTMC with a charge balancing counter anion complex 80. Despite the similar electrochemical and photophysical energy gaps for devices assembled from complexes 79 and 80, they emit red light at −3.6 and −5.6 V respectively, as the voltage is increased. Furthermore, the LEC containing complex 79 has a 60-fold faster Ton when compared to the cationic iTMC 80. This is presumably due to the faster delivery of the Na+ cations to the electrode (Fig. 26). Considering device lifetimes, complex 79 reached maximum brightness three times faster and displayed a 6-fold increase in its stability at a constant bias when compared to complex 80.232 For the development of efficient red emissive OLEDs, 1-(dibenzo[b,d]furan-4-yl)isoquinolinato-N,C3′(dbfiq), 5-isobutyryl-ppy and 5-acetyl-ppy-derived Ir(III) complexes were developed with high PLQY and good device performances.233–235 It was expected that complexes 81–83 comprising of dbfiq ligands together with rigid, extended π-conjugated frameworks would afford pure, saturated red emission with high quantum efficiency. Moreover, 1,3-bis(3,4-dibutoxyphenyl)propane-1,3-dionate-O,O (bdbp) was introduced into complex 81 to increase its solubility in organic solvents.233 Among these complexes, device based on complex 81 has shown the best performance with a Lmax of 7270 cd m−2 at 16.5 V and a ηext of 6.4%.233 Isobutyl substituents were introduced into the β-diketonate ligands to tune the band gap by lowering the energy levels of their LUMO frontier orbitals and to provide steric bulk in order to prevent close intermolecular interactions between neighboring molecules.235 In these systems, the electron-withdrawing carbonyl groups enhances the electron mobility and thus improves the charge balance in the emitting layers of the devices. Due to the increasing π-conjugation of the ligands in this series, complexes 84, 85, and 87 exhibit a bathochromic shift when compared to the PL spectra of complex 86. The introduction a 1-naphthyl substituent into the 5-isobutyryl-2-arylpyridine ligand of complex 85 afforded saturated red colors due to its narrower band gap. Devices doped with complex 86 displayed the best performances with Lmax of 35500 cd m−2 at 14 V and luminance and power efficiencies of 15.5 cd A−1 and 6.23 lm W−1 at 20 mA cm−2, respectively.235 Ir(III) complexes 88–93 containing a 5-acetyl-ppyhad ligand display suitable orange/red emission for OLED applications.234 To study how the solubility of the Ir complexes influences the efficiency of multi-layer solution-processed OLEDs, four Ir complexes with the same ligand core, 94–97, were synthesized.236 Studies of complex 94 reveal that the tert-butyl group significantly improves the solubility and thus it demonstrates the highest efficiency when fabricated into a device. Furthermore, by fine tuning the singlet and triplet energies of bipolar hosts based on indolocarbazole/1,3,5-triazine hybrids237 and (3′-(4,6-diphenyl-1,3,5-triazin-2-yl)-(1,1′-biphenyl)-3-yl)-9-carbazole (CzTrz)/tris(4-(9H-carbazol-9-yl)phenyl)amine (TCTA) to form an exciplex,238 OLEDs constructed with complex 94 have shown EQEs of 24.5 and 27%, respectively, which are some of the best values reported for orange PHOLEDs to-date.
 Fig. 25  Cyclic voltammograms of complexes 72–77 in tetra-n-butylammonium hexafluorophosphate (TBAP) at a scan rate of 100 mV s−1. Reproduced from ref. 230 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2009. 
 Fig. 26  Schematic distribution of the ionic species in the ionic Ir complex 80, left and the neutral Ir complex 79, right under a reverse bias. Reproduced from ref. 232 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2009. 
To optimize the energy transfer from the host to the guest material, carbazole and arylamine substituents have been appended to a ppy backbone and used for the synthesis of Ir(III) complexes 98–101, which were subsequently fabricated into OLEDs.239 Studies reveal that complexes with carbazole ligands have desirable phosphorescent properties due to the ability of the rigid carbazole group to promote light harvesting and facilitate efficient energy transfer to the guest molecules. In addition, chemical modification of the molecular structure of the ppy ligand can be carried out to increase the energy level of the HOMO, which in turn increases the hole-transporting abilities of the phosphorescent centers.
To survey the effects of having multiple π–π stacking domains within the coordination sphere of the Ir(III) center, the synthesis and characterization of a series of cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes, 102–109, have been reported.240 In order to prevent disadvantageous effects related to the presence of chlorido-impurities which can reduce the performance of the iTMC in LEC devices, the complexes were synthesized without using chloride dimer intermediates.241 Variable temperature solution 1H NMR and single crystal X-ray diffraction studies confirm that within the coordination sphere of the Ir(III) center, the pendant phenyl domains engage in multiple face-to-face π–π interactions (Fig. 27). All the complexes show superior luminescent properties, especially when used in thin solid films. LECs based on these complexes (i.e., with and without intra-cation π–π stacking interactions) afford very stable luminance outputs over time, even when driven at elevated current densities. Consequently, the device lifetime is significantly enhanced, reaching more than 2500 h under accelerated testing conditions for the most stable LEC. As shown in Table 5, the best performance was observed for devices containing complex 102, with a maximum efficiency of 3.5 cd A−1 and a luminance of 1024 cd m−2 (at an average current density of 300 A m−2), as well as an extrapolated lifetime in excess of 2800 h (the time to reach 50% of the maximum luminance). This is attributed to the presence of the phenyl substituents on the C^N ligand that reside on the periphery of the complex and are, thus, not involved in inter-ligand π–π stacking interactions close to the Ir(III) coordination sphere. In contrast to the above example, it is useful to note that despite the advantages of these interactions,245–247 this has not been used as a general design principle,248,249 since it does not ultimately improve the stability of the LED. Afterward, Suhr and co-workers designed a single-layer LEC with complex 102 as the emitter, along with an ionic LiPF6, additive to facilitate high device performance.244 The resulting devices exhibit modest lifetimes, reduced response times, with peak luminances of 5400 ± 100 cd m−2, which are 50% and 80% higher than the salt-free and unoptimized devices, respectively (Fig. 28). The enhanced performance is attributed to the blocking of reactive hydrogen species para to the Ir–N bonds of the cyclometalate ligands, as well as the preservation of steric bulk which reduces the self-quenching of the luminescence.250 The enhancement of the device efficiency can be attributed to both the smart design of the emissive complex and control of space charge effects at the device interfaces with ionic additives. In these condition, a more balanced carrier injection arises from superior cationic space charge at the cathode, which increase electron injection.73,251 The effect of the LiPF6 salt additive on the enhancement of device performance is clearly observed in Fig. 28.
 Fig. 27  The three intra-cation face-to-face π–π stacking interactions in complex 107: (a) in space-filling representations and (b) showing the two pyridyl/pendant phenyl interactions in red and blue and the cyclometalated phenyl/pendant phenyl interaction in green. (c) Emission spectra of powdered samples of 102–109 (λexc = 400 nm). (d) Emission behaviour (λexc = 365 nm) of complexes 102–109 in solution (top) and solid state (bottom). Reproduced from ref. 240 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. 
 Fig. 28  (a) Luminance vs. time plot of 102 LEEC devices under constant current driving with and without (pristine) 0.3% LiPF6. (b, top) EQE vs. time and (b, bottom) current efficiency vs. time of complex 102 LEC devices under constant current driving with and without (pristine) 0.3% LiPF6. (c) LEC device with structure ITO/PEDOT:PSS/102 + LiPF6/LiF/Al. Reproduced from ref. 244 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2016. 
Recently, Ir(III) complexes 110–112, were prepared to study the influence of an electron-withdrawing formyl group on the optoelectronic and phosphorescence properties of the resulting complexes. The complexes were fabricated into OLEDs via solution processing methods since all were reported to have excellent thermal stabilities.242 Although complex 100 had similar PL peaks to complex 102, its emission was blue-shifted because of the oxygen lone-pair of the ppy-CHO ligand lowers the energy level of the HOMO, affording a larger band gap. Following this work,242 Hierlinger and co-workers reported a new series of cationic Ir(III) complexes 113–117, in which the cyclometalate C^N ligands were modified with bulky substituents at the 4-position of the pyridine and/or the 3-position of the phenyl rings containing also dtbubpy (4,4′-di-tert-butyl-2,2′-bipyridine), to investigate the effect of sterics on both the photophysics and LEC device performance.243 Neat film and solution emission studies of these complexes revealed that the bulky substituents do not significantly affect the emission energies, Fig. 29. Among LEC devices based on complexes 113, 114, 1 and 115, the best current efficiency (7.4 cd A−1), highest luminance (737 cd m−2) and EQE (2.6%) was reported for complex 113. It is noteworthy that the reported EQE values, (1.2–2.6%), of these complexes are in line with the typical values for efficient yellow LECs. Furthermore, the cell efficiency is in agreement with the PLQY and EQE values of the film. In fact, the performance of the devices are 115 > 1 > 114, confirming how the quality of the thin films are strongly linked to the performance of the cells.
 Fig. 29  Emission spectra of complexes 1 and 113–117 at 298 K in degassed MeCN, with λexc = 400 nm. Reproduced from ref. 243 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2018. 
2.4 Ir(III) complexes containing ppy ligands bearing small substituents
Any new class of electroluminescent devices developed for full-color displays and illumination-quality lighting must have emissive properties in the visible region of the electromagnetic spectrum. The majority of iTMC-based devices are comprised of Ru or Os complexes, which emit in the red.252–257 Neutral complexes containing Ir(III) metal centers have also been used in OLEDs as emitters and are known to also emit efficiently in the red, green, and blue parts of the spectrum.258–260 In 2004, the ionic Ir(III) complex 1 was used for the first time to fabricate electroluminescent devices with yellow emission.47 Green emission has been reported by Slinker et al., for a single-layer device comprising of the ionic Ir(III) complex (118).261 Studies reveal that the mesomeric and inductive effects of the fluoro-substituent on the phenyl ring of the ligand are responsible for the blue-shifted emission.261ηext of up to 1.1 and 1.8% were achieved for devices containing Au and CsF/Al top contacts, respectively. The device efficiency varies in the same order as the work function (WF) of the contacts i.e. WFCsF/Al < WFITO < WFAu.261 Furthermore, addition of the ionic liquid (BMIP) produced a bias-dependent shift in the electroluminescence spectrum of this complex, significantly improving the response times of the device (Fig. 30).
 Fig. 30  Temporal evolution of the radiant flux in two ITO/118/Au devices, one as a pristine film and the other containing the IL (BMIP). Reproduced from ref. 261 with permission from the American Institute of Physics, copyright 2005. 
Electrochemical and luminescence studies of both neutral and ionic Ir(III) complexes reveal that using fluorinated cyclometalate ligands give rise to large HOMO–LUMO gaps, resulting in high-energy emission.209,262–264iTMCs have also been explored to drive electron-transfer reactions.265 Lowry et al. reported the synthesis of the iTMC 119, containing a multifluorinated cyclometalate ppy and a charge-transport enhancing dtbbpy ligand.266 The strongly electron-withdrawing fluoro-substituents on the 2-(2,4-difluorophenyl)-5-trifluoromethylpyridine ligand, or the dF(CF3)ppy make the metal cation more electron deficient, resulting in a significant increase in the metal-centered redox potential to almost 500 mV when compared to ppy. As seen in Fig. 31, for both 119 and 1, the LUMO is located on the π-system of the dtbbpy ligand, but the large HOMO–LUMO gap for complex 119 shifts the electroluminescence to higher energy which means that devices based on this complex emit blue-green light at λmax = 500 nm.266 Small chemical modifications to one of the simplest ionic Ir(III) heteroleptic complexes, 120, gave a PLQY of almost 100% for a solid film with green emission.267 The LEC based on this complex has external and power efficiencies of 15% and 40 lm W−1, respectively. These high external efficiencies are possible only if a near-quantitative internal electron-to-photon conversion occurs. The synthesis of the stable, green EL Ir tris-heteroleptic ionic complex 121, was also reported with a luminance of ∼1000 cd m−2 (Fig. 32), an efficiency of ∼3%, and a stability of approximately 55 h.268 The EL spectrum of LECs containing this complex lies between the LECs prepared from its two bis-heteroleptic counterparts, a result supported by both photophysical studies and quantum chemical calculations. It is noteworthy that the ηext of devices based on complexes 1, 106 and 105 showed the order 1 > 121 > 120, which is the reverse order of their PLQY values in solution, i.e.120 > 121 > 1.268 Additionally, the lifetime of the three devices also follows the order 1 > 121 > 120, indicating that the stability of the LECs is reduced as the number of fluorine substituents in the ligand increases.
 Fig. 31  Molecular orbital diagrams obtained from DFT calculations: (left) for complex 119, (right) complex 1 and (centre inset) redox potentials for the two complexes obtained through cyclic voltammetry. Reproduced from ref. 266 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2005. 
 Fig. 32  Luminance for a device containing complex 121. Reproduced from ref. 268 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2012. 
Despite their importance for tuning the emissive properties of coordination complexes, the bulky nature of the organic ligands and their ability to suppress self-quenching by sterically protecting the metal centers must also be carefully considered when designing new ligands for OLED applications. To investigate the influence of bulky ligands on reducing self-quenching, the cationic Ir(III) complexes 122 and 123, which contain sterically hindered, bulky, 4,5-diaza-9,9′-spirobifluorene (sb) ligands were designed.269 According to Fig. 33, the Ir center of complex 122 is coordinated by two cyclometalated ppy ligands and one sb ligand in a distorted octahedral geometry. The sb containing ligands increase the steric hindrance of the complexes that in turn decreases self-quenching. Photoluminescence studies reveal that both complexes display high EQEs in neat films.269 Cyclic voltammetry studies indicate that the HOMO energy level is reduced in these complexes due to the presence of the fluoro-substituents on the phenyl ring of the ppy ligand. This causes a blue shift in the emission of complex 123 when compared to the PL emission of the unsubstituted complex 122. Devices fabricated from single-layered neat films of complexes 122 and 123 possess high peak ηext and power efficiencies of 7.1% and 22.6 lm W−1 for complex 122, and 7.1% and 26.2 lm W−1 for complex 123.269 Moreover, the PL and EL properties of a host–guest cationic complex, employing the orange emissive complex 122 as the guest and the green-emissive complex 123 as the host, were also investigated.270 The results have shown that after optimization of the host–guest composition, the host–guest LECs had greatly enhanced quantum efficiencies of up to 10.4%, which represents a 1.5-times enhancement over their independent host and guest devices.270
 Fig. 33  Plot of the molecular structure of complex 122. H atoms are omitted for clarity. Reproduced from ref. 269 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2007. 
To gain further insight into the structure–property relationships of iTMCs, Hasan et al. reported studies on the family of cationic Ir(III) complexes 124–127, bearing 2,5-dipyridylpyrazine (2,5-dpp) and 2,2′:5′,2′′-terpyridine ancillary ligands.90 CV studies demonstrated that the extra nitrogen atom in the central pyrazine ring causes a +80 mV shift of the redox potentials of complexes 124 and 127. Devices based on complexes 124 and 126 display appreciable brightness and had good stabilities. A device based on complex 126 achieved the best performance which is consistent with the trend in predicted energies for their HOMOs namely, 126 (−5.76 eV) < 124 (−5.86 eV) < 125 (−6.05 eV) < 127 (−6.14 eV).90 In this respect, we can conclude that complex (111) had the lowest oxidation potential and thus is the most efficient at facilitating hole injection. One of the principal problems of LECs is the lack of true blue-emission in cationic coordination complexes which is required for full color and white-light-emission for display applications. In an attempt to prepare a blue light-emitting LEC, the wide-bandgap Ir(III) complex 128 was prepared. Studies reveal that this complex displays blue light emission with a λmax of 476 nm when photoexcited in a thin film, with a PLQY of 52%.271 DFT calculations suggest that at least three low-energy triplet states exist for this complex within a narrow energy window (Fig. 34). Due to the different nature of these states, their geometries are not equivalent and hence their relaxation to the ground state is accompanied by the emission of photons of different energies. In this respect, only one triplet state results in green light emission, while the other two give emissions in the blue region of the spectrum. Nevertheless, the electroluminescence spectrum of complex 128 was shifted by 80 nm relative to its photoluminescence spectrum, resulting in the emission of green light, which is probably due to the high concentration of iTMC within the thin film.271
 Fig. 34  Energy diagram showing the relative energies, electronic character and vertical emission wavelengths calculated for the lowest triplet states of complex 128. Reproduced from ref. 271 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. 
Replacing the 3,4,7,8-tetramethyl-phen ancillary ligand with 4,7-diphenyl-phen in complex 128 affords the new complex 129. A LEC based on complex 129 emits yellow-green electroluminescence at λmax = 533 nm, with maximum luminance and a current density of 330 cd m−2 and 15.57 mA cm−2 at 10 V, respectively.272 The effect of two different imidazolium-based ILs, BMIP and 1-ethyl-3-methylimidazolium hexafluorophosphate (EMIP), on the active layer also show that the luminance and current density of these devices increase with increasing ionic conductivity.
Chen et al. achieved a longer lifetime in LEC devices through the rational design of cationic Ir complexes, 130–132. These complexes contain sterically hindered sb, N∧N ancillary ligands, bearing one or two pendant phenyl groups namely, 3-phenyl-sb (131) and 3,6-diphenyl-sb (132), together with an electron-deficient cyclometalated C∧N ligand, to foster optimal intramolecular co-facial π–π interactions.273 The X-ray crystal structures of complexes 131 and 132 demonstrate strong intramolecular face-to-face π–π stacking interactions between the pendant phenyl ring and the difluorophenyl ring of the cyclometalate ligands with distances of 3.38 Å for complex 131 and 3.40 and 3.46 Å for complex 132, Fig. 35. This leads the authors to conclude that the significant longer lifetime of LEC devices based on complex 131 is due to an increase in the π–π stacking interactions that serves to minimize the expansion of the metal–ligand bonds in the excited state. In this respect, a 4-fold lifetime enhancement was observed for a device based on complex 131, compared to complex 130 without the pendant phenyl substituent. It is worth noting that the device lifetimes of complexes 130–132 were 141, 585 and 102 min, respectively (Fig. 36). In general, the iTMCs used in LECs have molecular weights <1000 g mol−1, which afford good solubilities in a wide range organic solvents. However, good solubilities can also sometimes hamper the formation of thin films. The quality of such films can subsequently be improved via the addition of small amounts of an inactive polymer or an IL.
 Fig. 35  Face-to-face intramolecular π–π stacking interactions between the pendant phenyl ring and the cyclometalated difluorophenyl ring of complex 131 (left) and complex 132 (right). The PF6− counterion and hydrogen atoms are omitted for clarity. Reproduced from ref. 273 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2012. 
 Fig. 36  Time dependence of (a) brightness (filled symbols) and current density (empty symbols); (b) EQE (filled symbols) and the corresponding power efficiency (empty symbols) for complexes 130–132. Reproduced from ref. 273 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2012. 
In this respect it could also be anticipated that the stability of LECs could be enhanced via the covalent attachment of iTMCs to polymer chains. The first example of a polymeric LEC contained the Ir(III) complex 133, affording devices with higher stabilities when compared to those fabricated from small molecular-weight complexes.96 This complex has been chemically tuned to emit in the deep-red of the visible spectrum through the introduction of electron-withdrawing ester substituents on the bpy ligand. The enhanced stabilities and longer emission wavelengths are attributed to a uniform distribution of active iTMCs in complex 134 because of their chemical linkage to the polymer backbone. Red phosphorescent emitters for OLEDs were also synthesized from Ir(III) complexes 135–140, containing 5-benzoyl-2-phenylpyridine ligands. Fluorine and methyl substituents were introduced to chemically tune the band gaps of these complexes by adjusting the energy levels of their HOMO and LUMO frontier orbitals.274 Studies demonstrated that structural modifications to the organic ligands of phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes can improve the EL performances of the corresponding OLEDs. In later work, tuning the yellow-green to orange colors of the triplet emitters 141–144 with enhanced charge injection/charge transporting features was achieved via the functionalization of both polypyridyl ancillary ligands and the phenyl ring of their ppy ligands.275 The EL spectra of these complexes have shown a negligible voltage dependence, resulting in improved color stabilities as the voltage was increased. Moreover, functionalizing the ppy ligands with an electron-withdrawing dicyanovinyl substituent, revealed that the emission energy of the resulting Ir complexes 145–149 strongly depends on both the position and the electronic nature of the substituents. These changes facilitated broad tuning of the photoluminescence properties from blue/green to deep red.276 It also leads to the discovery of a highly efficient blue OLED containing FIrpic (150) doped into CDBP host, highlighting that the selection of the host material is an important consideration when optimizing the emission efficiency of OLEDs.277 In comparison with the CBP thin film, which has shown a complicated non-exponential transient photoluminescence decay curve, the CDBP thin film displays a mono-exponential decay curve with a relatively long lifetime of 1.4 μs (Fig. 37). As part of a study to develop blue-emitting materials, Seo et al. reported a family of highly efficient, deep-blue phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes based on picolinic acid N-oxide (151 and 152),278 acetylacetone, biimidazole (153–155),279 and picolinato (156 and 157)280 ancillary ligands. These new Ir(III) complexes were thermally and electrochemically stable with excellent photoluminescence efficiencies of over 80%.278 As previously described, the energy levels of these complexes were optimized with the introduction of electron-donating and electron-withdrawing substituents onto their ppy ligand. A high performance OLED fabricated from complex 152 provided a high ηext (23.3%) with deep-blue emission and CIE coordinates of (0.15, 0.21) (Fig. 38).278 The CF3 substituents on the phenylpyridine-based ligand played an important role in tuning the electronic states of the complex, resulting in a hypsochromic shift of the emission spectra.278,280 The subsequent introduction of a hexylcarbazolyl substituent facilitated energy transfer from the carbazole units to the Ir(III) cores of the heteroleptic cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes 153–157, resulting in an increase in luminous intensity without any change in the mission color.279,280 Moreover, the conjugation effect due to the presence of a fluorine substituent on the 3-position of the phenyl ring of the ppy ligand (158–160) increases the HOMO energy level when compared to the 2- and 4-fluorinated ppy analogues with lower HOMOs.281 Furthermore, it has also been shown that replacement of electron-donating with electron-withdrawing substituents on the ortho-metalated phenyl ring affords blue emission, and increases the oxidation potential of the complex.304 However, for use in blue OLEDs, cyclometalated Ir complexes 161 and 162 have lower HOMOs in the presence of a commonly used host and display higher performances when compared to the standard FIrpic emitter. Devices comprising of 161 possess excellent color stabilities, excellent electroluminescence properties and very low efficiency roll-offs of about 1% at 1000 cd m−2.282
 Fig. 37  (a) Transient photoluminescence decay for CDBP and CBP films doped with 3% FIrpic, and (b) alignment of triplet energy levels for FIrpic, CDBP, and CBP. Reproduced from ref. 277 with permission from the American Institute of Physics, copyright 2003. 
 Fig. 38  The ηext of devices comprising of the following: Type I: ITO/2-TNATA/TAPC/151 or 152:mCP/151 or 152:UGH2/BAlq/LiF/Al, Type II: ITO/2-TNATA/TAPC/TCTA/151 or 152:mCP/151 or 152:UGH2/BAlq/LiF/Al. Reproduced from ref. 278 with permission from the Elsevier, copyright 2010. 
A substantial improvement in the performance of OLEDs was achieved for Ir complexes containing tetraphenylimidodiphosphinate (tpip) ancillary ligands.283–285,287 Zhu et al. reported the synthesis of two bis-cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes (163 and 164). These complexes were designed as green or blue-green emitters, with short excitation lifetimes that are capable of electron transport. The goal was to ultimately exploit these compounds for phosphorescent OLEDs with low efficiency roll-offs (Fig. 39).283 Tpip with its polar PO bond has a short excitation lifetime that in turn affects the emission wavelengths of the complexes. For devices based on complex 163, the efficiency roll-off from 100 to 1000 cd m−2 was only 11.88%. Devices incorporating complexes 163 and 163 have shown good EL performances, which are attributed to their carrier transport properties and the short lifetimes of their excited states.
 Fig. 39  Top: Oak Ridge thermal ellipsoidal plot (ORTEP) of complexes 163 and 164. Thermal ellipsoids are plotted at the 50% probability level. Hydrogen atoms are omitted for clarity. Bottom: Intermolecular π–π stacking interactions between neighbouring molecules of 164. Reproduced from ref. 283 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2011. 
In a correlated study, a series of Ir(III) complexes, 165–170, with tpip ancillary ligands were synthesized to investigate how the substitution pattern and number of trifluoromethyl substituents on the ppy ligands effects OLED performance.284 Complex 170 with two meta CF3 substituents displays higher oxidation potentials and its emission spectrum undergoes a bathochromic shift of 25 nm compared to complex 165. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) and differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) of complexes 165–170 revealed good thermal stabilities up to 380 °C, suggesting that all the six complexes are suitable for the fabrication of thin films, where purification by sublimation methods are feasible.284Fig. 40 illustrates the EL spectra, power efficiency, current efficiency versus J curves and voltage–luminance characteristics for each device. The Von for devices of complexes 166–170 are in the range 3.5–3.9 V (Fig. 40(d)). Amongst this series of compounds, a device fabricated from complex 168 displays the best performance with a maximum luminance of over 39000 cd m−2 (Fig. 40(d)). As expected, the substitution patterns of the three fluorine atoms appended to the phenyl ring of the ppy ligand also affects the emission properties of the complexes 171–173. The application of the Htpip ligand improves the overall electron mobility of the complexes, making them comparable to the popular electron transport material Alq3 at the same electric fields.285 The EL efficiency roll-off properties at high current density in all of the devices were low, which is beneficial compared to OLEDs with high efficiencies and brightness levels. It is interesting to note that introducing CF3 groups into both the ppy and Htpip ligands of complex 168, increases the electron mobility of the device that had a high ηp of 113.23 lm W−1 and a CE of 115.39 cd A−1 (174).286 Moreover, the same trends were also observed when ortho and para F/CF3 substituents were introduced into the ppy and tpip ligands,287 affording complexes 175–177. All the devices fabricated from these complexes maintained high efficiencies at high brightness (more than 40000 cd m−2), signifying their usefulness for practical applications that include lighting. Furthermore, devices containing the new bipolar host material, 4,4′-(6-(thiophen-2-yl)-1,3,5-triazine-2,4-diyl)bis(N,N-diphenylaniline), or TTT, afforded onset voltages between 2.9 V to 3.0 V, which is about 0.5 V less than those of structurally similar OLEDs with an mCP host.305
 Fig. 40  Performances of devices based on complexes 166–170: (a) EL spectra at different current densities. (b) Power and (c) current efficiency as a function of current density. (d) Luminance vs. voltage. Reproduced from ref. 284 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2013. 
Two blue-emitting cationic Ir(III) complexes 178 and 179 containing 2,2′-biimidazole ancillary ligands were subsequently synthesized. These complexes have good solubilities in organic solvents and were capable of forming good thin films.288 Again, the introduction of electron-withdrawing CF3 substituents onto the 3-position of the phenyl ring of their ppy ligands increases the energy gap between their HOMO and LUMO frontier orbitals, leading to a hypsochromic shift when compared to the parent Ir(III) complexes.288 This shift was similar to that observed for the blue-emitting soft salt-based Ir(III) complex, [(F2CH3-ppy)2Ir(dppmp)],306 (where dppmp = 2-[(diphenylphosphino)methyl]pyridine). Studies revealed that complexes 178 and 179 are thermally stable and good candidates for blue phosphors in solution-processed OLEDs. Furthermore, it was also shown that biimidazole-type N,N′ ancillary ligands can invoke a significant destabilization of the LUMO energies of the three complexes, [Ir(dFppy)2(H2biim)](PF6), [Ir(dFppy)2(dMebiim)](PF6) and [Ir(dFppy)2(o-xylbiim)](PF6), (where H2biim, dMebiim and o-xylbiim are 1H,1′H-2,2′-biimidazole, 1,1′-dimethyl-2,2′biimidazole and 1,1′-(α,α′-o-xylylene)-2,2′-biimidazole, respectively).307 Unfortunately, the poor solubilities of these complexes prohibited them from being used in solution-processed devices. However, aryl-substitution of the diimine ligand and the mesityl groups of the ppy ligand was able to significantly improve the solution-state photoluminescence quantum efficiencies, solubilities and quenching processes.193,308,309 Following this strategy, two cationic green- to blue-emitting, fluorinated Ir complexes, 180 and 181, were studied. Both complexes possess highly efficient, deep blue emission in both MeCN solution and PMMA thin films.289 He et al. carried out systematic studies on blue-green, red and white OLEDs containing cationic Ir complexes with imidazole-type ancillary ligands.216,290,291,310 The latter can be easily prepared and able to blue-shift the emission spectra of the resulting complexes.290 By tuning the conjugation of the imidazole-type ligands, LECs based on the cationic Ir complexes 182–187 have shown blue-green (497 nm) to red (650 nm) electroluminescence, Fig. 41.
 Fig. 41  EL spectra of ITO/PEDOT:PSS/182–187:BMIP/Al. Reproduced from ref. 290 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2009. 
High efficiencies of 8.4, 18.6, and 13.2 cd A−1 were attained for the resulting blue-green, yellow, and orange emitting devices, with ηext of 3.4%, 6.0%, and 6.1%, respectively. White LECs were obtained by doping the red-emissive complex 186 into the active layer of a blue-green LEC based on complex 183. The resulting white LECs provided warm-white light with CIE coordinates of (0.42, 0.44), and color-rendering indexes (CRI) of up to 81, and a peak power efficiency of 10 lm W−1.290 In addition to their use in LECs, these complexes are also promising phosphorescent dopants for solution-processed OLEDs.310 Complexes containing ppy ligands were found to show better device performances than their 2-(2,4-difluorophenyl)pyridyl counterparts. Studies also revealed that the ionic nature of these complexes is beneficial for reducing the aggregation of the dopants in the polar host, which results in less severe TTA at high current densities.216,310 Cationic Ir complexes 188 and 189, with N-heterocyclic carbene–pyridine ancillary ligands (Fig. 42) also exhibit blue light emission in CH3CN solution, attributed to the destabilization of their LUMO orbitals.291 Devices based on 189 have shown inferior electroluminescent properties versus188, due to their lower HOMOs as a consequence of the electron withdrawing fluorine substituents on the cyclometalated ancillary ligands.291
 Fig. 42  Plot of the molecular structures of complexes 188 (a) and 189 (b). The PF6 counter anions and solvent molecules are omitted for clarity. Reproduced from ref. 291 with permission from the Elsevier, copyright 2012. 
Recently, Sunesh et al. reported cationic Ir(III) complexes 190–197, containing imidazole ancillary ligands, as light-emitting complexes for LECs.292,293 Imidazole-based ancillary ligands destabilize the LUMOs of heteroleptic Ir complexes, shifting their emissive properties to the blue region of the visible spectrum. LECs fabricated from complexes 190 and 191 exhibited green and blue-green electroluminescence, respectively.292 Although tuning the colors of their emission was successfully achieved, they have shown maximum current efficiencies of only 1.0 cd A−1 and 0.88 cd A−1 respectively, attributed to the presence of strong intermolecular interactions between neighboring molecules within the film.
To reduce these interactions, imidazole-based ancillary ligands bearing alkyl substituents with a range of chain lengths were developed, affording complexes 192–197.293 Although increasing the length of the alkyl chain did not significantly shift the emission wavelengths of the complexes, the luminance and efficiencies of LECs based on these complexes were enhanced with the increase of the alkyl chains length. Consequently, a high luminance of 7309 cd m−2 and current efficiency of 3.85 cd A−1 was obtained for LECs containing complex 196, where the intermolecular interactions were significantly reduced, which in turn suppressed the self-quenching. By successfully combining substituent and ancillary ligand effects in these systems, a series of interesting blue-phosphorescent Ir complexes 198–209 were subsequently synthesized, and a systematic control of the their HOMO/LUMO frontier orbitals was carried out to target complexes with large HOMO–LUMO energy gaps of −2.76 to −3.06 eV.294–296 The introduction of both fluorine and CF3 groups into the phenyl rings of the ancillary ligands shifted their emission to the blue region.295 These ligands are readily prepared via the facile iodination of difluorophenylpyridine derivatives following the synthetic pathway presented in Fig. 43.
 Fig. 43  Synthetic pathway for the preparation of fluorinated ligands present in complexes 198–209. Reproduced from ref. 295 with permission from the Chemical Society of Japan, copyright 2006. 
The introduction of a methyl group into the phenyl ring of the ancillary ligand, 2-pyridyltriazolate resulted in rare, deep-blue phosphorescence at room temperature, where its emission bands were blue-shifted when compared to FIrpic.277 Efficient, deep-blue electrophosphorescence was also obtained using the charge-trapping phosphorescent complex, 198, that was doped into a range of wide-energy-gap hosts that include, diphenyldi(o-tolyl)silane (UGH1) and p-bis(triphenylsilyly)benzene (UGH2). For these systems, exciton formation was reported to occur directly on the guest molecules.294Fig. 44 illustrates the performance of OLEDs based on UGH1 and UGH2, co-deposited with complex 198 with ηext values of 8.8 and 11.6%, respectively. Following this strategy, 4-substituted-2′-pyridyltriazoles were designed to serve as ancillary ligands in addition to the main ppy ligand in the heteroleptic Ir(III) complexes 203–209, which also display deep blue phosphorescence.296 By considering the effects of the substituents on the energies of the frontier orbitals, these complexes were designed to display emissive properties in the blue region, where λmax = 448–464 nm, depending on the electronic nature of the substituents at the C-4 position of the pyridine ring of the ancillary ligand. Furthermore, the introduction of an electron-withdrawing CF3 group to the C-3 position of the phenyl ring of the ppy ligand significantly lowers the emission wavelength by 8 nm.296 A comparison of the I–V–L curves and current efficiencies of OLEDs based on complexes 207 and 198 reveals that the device based on complex 207 emits deep blue light corresponding to a CIE (0.15, 0.18), which is quite close to the ideal value of (0.15, 0.15) (Fig. 45).296 Unfortunately, the emission efficiency of this device is lower than its counterpart, complex 198. In addition to these two complexes, Ir(III) complexes incorporating a tetrazole pyridine ligand, 210 and 211, have also been developed.297 Manipulation of the molecular structures of the organic ligands, including functionalization of the cyclometalate ancillary ligands were achieved, affording phosphorescence emitters that have been used for the fabrication of blue phosphorescent OLEDs. Complexes containing 2-(2,4-difluorophenyl)-4-methylpyridine (dfmppy) and ppy were thermally more stable than their 2,4-diphenylquinoline-based counterparts. In this respect, the introduction of a methyl group into the pyridine ring of the dfmppy significantly lowers the energy of the HOMO, resulting in blue emission.
 Fig. 44  Quantum (circles) and power (squares) efficiency vs. drive current density for UGH1-based (closed symbols) or UGH2-based (open symbols) devices. Inset: EL at 10 mA cm−2 originating solely from complex 198 in UGH1 (broken line) and UGH2 (solid line). Reproduced from ref. 294 with permission from the American Institute of Physics, copyright 2003. 
 Fig. 45  (top) Luminescence efficiencies and CIE coordinates for devices containing complexes 198 and 207; (bottom) I–V–L curves for devices containing complexes 198 and 207. Reproduced from ref. 296 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2013. 
An interesting alternative to phenyl-1,2,4-triazole is the 1,4-disubstituted-1H-1,2,3-triazole ligand. These compounds are facile to prepare311,312via “click chemistry” and as a consequence, they have been widely used as cyclometalate ligands in both neutral and cationic Ir(III) complexes.313–316 Representative examples reported by Fernández-Hernández et al. are the neutral bis- and tris-cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes (212 and 213) containing 1-benzyl-4-(2,6-difluorophenyl)-1H-1,2,3-triazole (dfptrBn) ligands.298 Studies reveal that complexes fac-212 and fac-213, were emissive in both solution and thin films, with emission quantum yields of 76% and colors in the blue to blue-green region. Due to the presence of the fluorine substituents that lower their HOMO, the emission of complex fac-213 was blue-shifted compared to complex fac-212. Moreover, in both cases, the emission was blue-shifted compared to their homoleptic arylpyridine Ir(III) counterparts, fac-[Ir(ppy)3] and fac-[Ir(dfppy)3] (dfppy = 2-(2,4-difluorophenyl)pyridine).263,317 The photophysical and electrochemical properties of these complexes were further investigated by DFT calculations, which supported the experimental data.
Given their predictable band assignments, high electron affinities, high PLQY, and good thermal and chemical stabilities, 1,3,4-oxadiazole derivatives have also received considerable attention recently for applications as electroactive emissive materials. For example, four heteroleptic Ir(III) complexes, 214–217, were designed and synthesized containing cyclometalated 4-trifluoromethyl phenylpyridine, together with 2-(5-phenyl-1,3,4-oxadiazol-2-yl)-phenol and F/CF3-substituted derivatives, as the ancillary ligands.299 The presence of two CF3 groups on the meta-positions of the phenyl ring of the ppy ancillary ligand was effective to tune the emission of the complexes towards the red region. Electrochemical studies on these complexes revealed that, although the introduction of F/CF3 groups has little effect on the energy levels of the frontier orbitals, they do however confer electrochemical stability on the Ir(III) complexes.299 This observations were further supported by DFT calculations, (Fig. 46).299
 Fig. 46  Left: Cyclic voltammograms for complexes 214–217 in CH2Cl2; right: calculated energy levels for frontier orbitals of complexes 214–217 at the B3LYP/6-31G**; LANL2DZ theory level. Reproduced from ref. 299 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2014. 
As already mentioned, an important challenge for the fabrication of full-color displays and white-light sources is the design and development of pure-blue-to-deep-blue-emitting ionic phosphors. In this respect, deep-blue emission in neutral Ir(III) complexes, 218 and 219, functionalized with substituted pyridyl cyclometalate or azolate chromophores has been achieved by using a non-conjugated phosphine ancillary ligand, containing a methylene unit as the electronic blocker.300,318 On the one hand, the π-acceptor character of the PPh2 group increases the associated metal-to-ligand charge-transfer (MLCT) energy and destabilizes the d–d excited states.299 On the other hand, the saturated nature of the PPh2 group confines the electronic transition dipole around the dfppy unit in the 2-(4,6-difluorophenyl)-4-tert-butylpyridine (dfpbpy), 5-(2-pyridyl)-3-(trifluoromethyl)pyrazole (fppz), and 3-(trifluoromethyl)-5-(2-pyridyl)-1,2,4-triazole (fptz) chromophores.299 This work opened up a new strategy for the realization of OLEDs with high-efficiency blue and true-blue phosphorescence based on complexes 218 and 219. These rarely observed properties, supported by photophysical and computational studies,300 revealed that in addition to the balance of carriers and the confinement of excitons, the development of host materials with high triplet energies are also important for high-efficiency, blue to deep-blue OLEDs. Using the non-conjugated 1-[(diphenylphosphino)methyl]-3-methylimidazolin-2-ylidene-C,C2′ (dppmmi) as the ancillary ligand, Zhang et al. reported three new cationic Ir(III) complexes, [Ir(ppy)2(dppmmi)](PF6) (220), and [Ir(dfppy)2(dppmmi)](PF6) (221) (Fig. 47).301 The dppmmi ligand bearing both PPh2 and imidazolium carbene moieties is a strong field ligand that can shift the emission of the complexes further into the blue. The non-conjugated, 2,4-difluorobenzyl-N-pyrazole (dfbpz) was used as the cyclometalated ligand for [Ir(dfbpz)2(pymbi)](PF6) (pymbi is 3-methyl-1-(2-pyridyl)benzimidazolin-2-ylidene-C,C2′), which was prepared for comparison with complexes 220, 221, and [Ir(dfppz)2(dppmmi)](PF6).
 Fig. 47  Top: ORTEP plots of the crystal structures of complexes 220 and 221. PF6− counter ions, solvent molecules, and hydrogen atoms are omitted for clarity. Thermal ellipsoids are drawn at 50% probability. Bottom: Molecular structures of complexes 220 and 221 showing how functionalization of the cyclometalate ligand shifts the emissive properties towards the blue region. Reproduced from ref. 301 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2014. 
Studies reveal that pure blue to deep blue light emission at short wavelengths can be achieved by using a chromophoric ligand.301 In this respect, solution-processed OLEDs containing complexes 220 and 221 display blue-green and blue electroluminescence with λmax values of 498 and 478 nm, and maximum current efficiencies of 3.8 and 3.4 cd A−1, respectively.301 Recently, another interesting study was reported by Shavaleev et al.302 concerning complexes 222 and 223, which contain sulfur pentafluoride-modified 1-phenylpyrazole together with ppy, cyclometalate ligands. These complexes emit bright-green phosphorescence in both acetonitrile and when solution processed into thin films under ambient conditions.302 Their emission maxima range from 482 nm to 519 nm with high PLQY values between 70 and 79%.302 SF5 is a bulky, chemically inert, polar, hydrophobic, electron-withdrawing group that has been introduced into the molecular structures of cyclometalated ancillary ligands.302 Notably, SF5 is less reactive and more electron-withdrawing than F and CF3, so it increases the oxidation potential and the HOMO–LUMO gap in the resulting Ir(III) complexes. These complexes therefore display phosphorescence that is blue shifted when compared to their fluoro and CF3 counterparts. Furthermore, the bulky SF5 group enhances the solubility of the complexes, preventing close intermolecular interactions between neighboring complexes in the solid state.302 Despite these advantages, the irreversible reduction potential of SF5 can be problematic for the resulting electronic devices. For example, complexes 222 and 223 display electroluminescence in OLEDs, but not in LECs. Nevertheless, these complexes show green to yellow-green electroluminescence in doped multilayer OLEDs with CIE coordinates of (0.25–0.29, 0.44–0.54) (Fig. 48) and with ηext of up to 1.7% in the solution-processed devices.
 Fig. 48  Crystal structure, and CIE coordinates of a device based on complex 222. Reproduced from ref. 302 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 
Novel blue phosphorescent bis- and tris-cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes 227–230, bearing benzoyl substituents were also developed, where the benzoyl group was used as an electron-withdrawing substituent to target the realization of complexes with blue-shifted phosphorescence.303 The homoleptic tris-cyclometalated complex has shown the largest blue-shifted photoluminescence, emitting sky blue with a PLQY of 0.90.303 Electrochemical and DFT studies of this complex reveal that its blue-shifted PL and EL properties are mostly due to the stabilization of its HOMO.303
2.5 Homoleptic Ir(III) complexes containing ppy ligands
Highly efficient LEDs with multilayer structures were fabricated by doping the neutral, green electrophosphorescent complex [Ir(ppy)3] (231),319–321 into various electron-transport layer (ETL) hosts.258,322,323 In this respect, the search for an appropriate host material is of critical importance for the discovery of high-efficiency triplet-emitting OLED devices. For devices containing CBP electron transport layers, ηext and power efficiencies of only 8.0% and 31 lm W−1, respectively, were obtained.258 These performances were attributed to the efficient electron transfer processes involving both the singlet and triplet excited states of the Ir(ppy)3 host.258 In addition, the short phosphorescent decay time of the complex 231 decreases the saturation of the phosphor with increasing drive currents, leading to a maximum luminance of 100000 cd m−2.258 In contrast to CBP, devices fabricated from 3-phenyl-4-(1′-naphthyl)-5-phenyl-1,2,4-triazole (TAZ) hosts displayed a maximum ηext of 15.4% and a luminous power efficiency of 40 lm W−1.322 Generally, both devices have shown a gradual decrease in their quantum efficiencies with increasing current, attributed to triplet–triplet annihilation (TTA) that is present in all electrophosphorescent devices.
The synthesis and characterization of a series of highly efficient LED materials based on fluorinated organometallic Ir(III) complexes, 232–245, have also been reported. In these systems, the electroluminescence can be optimized by chemically modifying the nature and position of the substituents on the aromatic rings of their organic ligands.324 In this respect, the wavelength of the emission band of the LEDs were tuned from λmax = 500 to 595 nm by changing the position and tuning the stereo-electronics of the aforementioned substituents (Fig. 49).324 These changes are probably due to the strong π-donating effects of both F and MeO groups, which may interact with either the Ir(III) metal or the pyridine ring leading to a destabilization of the HOMO localized on the Ir metal and stabilization of the LUMO on the pyridine ligand.
 Fig. 49  Normalized EL spectra of Ir complexes 231–245. Reproduced from ref. 324 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2001. 
Introducing bulky pinene substituents into the ppy ligand of complex 246, served to minimize bimolecular interactions that subsequently reduced self-quenching in the designed OLED.325 Complex 246 displays very strong green phosphorescence with a PLQY of 0.71 in solution and a relative short triplet lifetime of 0.33 μs in the solid state. By increasing the concentration of the complex, a gradual increase in brightness and ηext was observed, further confirming that aggregation quenching is almost negligible in the phosphorescence device.325 In addition, it was also shown that Ir(III) complexes 247–249 containing ppy ligands bearing fluorene substituents are also suitable for the fabrication of single-layer LEDs.326 The degree of conjugation of the organic ligand permits the wavelength of their emission to be tuned, and the ligand guest environment controls the degree of solid-state fluorescence quenching. The ligands in these compounds therefore provide different levels of steric protection around the metal centers,326 facilitating studies on the effect of ligand substitution patterns on their solid-state phosphorescence bulk morphology. Atomic force microscopy (AFM) studies indicate that smooth films with an average mean roughness of ca. 0.3 nm could be achieved by casting these complexes directly from toluene.326 Additionally, Gong et al. reported the first high-efficiency single-layer polymer phosphorescent light-emitting diodes (PPLEDs) containing complex 247 that was doped into a PVK:PBD matrix.327 The resulting single-layer device exhibited a ηext value of 10% and an ηp of 3 lm W−1.327 Increasing the concentration of complex 247 increased the operating voltage of the electrophosphorescence, which subsequently afforded a low ηp due to severe charge-trapping on the emitter.327 To fabricate highly efficient red-emissive OLEDs, Tsuboyama et al. prepared a series of facial homoleptic cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes 250–252, that display highly efficient (0.19–0.29) red phosphorescence.328 One of the challenges in this field is to develop new red-emissive metal complexes with large PLQY. In this respect, the introduction of substituents and/or large conjugated aromatic moieties into the framework of the organic ligands causes a shift of the wavelength emission of the OLEDs to the red compared to devices based on the unsubstituted, well-known Ir(ppy)3.328 OLEDs based on members of the Ir isoquinoline family have shown pure-red emission spectra with λmax > 620 nm.328 An OLED device containing complex 252 as the phosphorescent dopant exhibited very high electroluminescence efficiencies, as well as distinctly saturated red emission,328 which is very close to the ideal red specification proposed by the National Television Standards Committee (NTSC).328 In order shift the energy of Ir(ppy)3 from blue to red, electron-donating groups were introduced onto the pyridine ring of the ligand to decrease the energy of the HOMO. For example, functionalization of the ppy ligand with methyl groups influences both the electrochemical and photophysical properties of the resulting complex.329–332 The crystal structure and DFT calculations of complex fac-253, which contains a methyl substituted ppy ligand reveal that the non-co-planar arrangement between the phenyl and pyridine rings, very likely influences the energy of the MLCT excited state, as well as the intermolecular interactions in the solid state.329,330 A simple procedure was developed to prepare the fac and mer isomers of the novel Ir complex 254, comprising of 2-phenyl-4-methylpyridine ligands.331 The results reveal that introducing a methyl group into the para-position of the ppy ligand causes a blue-shift in the emission wavelength, further confirming that the emission color is also tunable compared to the position of the substituents in meridional isomers.331 The presence of methyl groups at various positions of the ppy ligands in complexes 255–259 has also been shown to significantly influence the absorption, emission, redox potentials and electroluminescence.332 The PLQYs of these complexes increase in the order: 235 > 236 > Ir(ppy)3 > 258 > 257 > 259. In addition, complex 256 with methyl groups at the 4- and 4′-positions has shown a significantly improved ηext, which for OLEDs was 11%, i.e., nearly 200% higher than the value obtained for the benchmark [Ir(ppy)3].332 Moreover, the addition of a tert-butyl group in complex 260 significantly suppresses the decay of the device efficiency at high current density.333 A PPLED device containing complex 260, doped with poly(2-(6-cyano-6-methyl)-heptyloxy-1,4-phenylene) (CNPPP) also possessed a larger efficiency (7.07 cd A−1 and 942 cd m−2) compared to similar devices based on Ir(ppy)3.333 This improvement is attributed to the better interaction of the guest molecules of complex 260 with the conjugated polymer host, thereby preventing phase segregation and improving the effectiveness of the energy transfer process.333 In order to gain more insight into PPLEDs, single-layer PPLEDs based on the methyl-substituted Ir complex (261) were later fabricated by appropriate thermal treatment, using efficient bilayer cathodes and a suitably modified layer composition.334 These devices show that the driving field was independent of the concentration of complex 261, which means that the build-up of space-charge due to the trapping of holes in the emissive layer is insignificant. In other words, the build-up of space-charge is mostly prevented by providing a sufficiently large number of electrons via efficient injection, transport, and recombination in optimized devices. Consequently, these devices demonstrated low diving voltages and ηp values of up to 14.1 lm W−1 (Fig. 50). Since hole injection represents the limiting process in these devices, to facilitate hole injection, Yang et al. studied devices with a single cross-linked hole-transporting layer comprising of (X-HTL) QUPD (N4,N4′-bis(4-((6-((3-ethyloxetan-3-yl)methoxy)hexyl)oxy)phenyl)-N4,N4′-bis(4-methoxyphenyl)-[1,1′-biphenyl]-4,4′-diamine).335 The introduction of X-HTL double layers together with a bis-spirobifluorene host doped with the soluble green emitter 262, resulted in a three-fold efficiency increase, when compared to devices without X-HTL, while maintaining the low driving voltage and stable green emission.336 The authors concluded that the X-HTL concept is able to significantly increase the power efficiency of blue-light-emitting electrophosphorescent devices.336
 Fig. 50  (a) Current–brightness–voltage characteristics of a device based on complex 261; (b) luminance efficiency and ηp as a function of the current density. Reproduced from ref. 334 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2004. 
According to Samuel et al., Ir-based phosphorescent dendrimers can be highly efficient light-emitting materials for OLEDs.337–339,341,342,344,345,352,353 Light-emitting dendrimers comprise of surface groups, dendrons, and cores. Their modular construction permits their processability and electronic properties to be independently tuned, tolerating a wide range of chromophores, which in many cases would be unsuitable for isolated use, or exist as the repeat unit of a polymer.337 A methodology for the preparation of solution-processible dendrimers, 263 and 264, with fac-tris(ppy)Ir-cores was developed by Markham et al.337 The resulting compounds were fabricated into single layer OLEDS via spin coating techniques that display very-high-efficiency green phosphorescence.337 The high efficiency is attributed to the uniform nature of the film, as well as the high PLQY of the dendrimer blend, and the efficient injection of charges at the anode and cathode.337 Very high efficiencies were also achieved for two-layer OLEDs that contained a similar dendrimer layer comprising of complex 263, together with a second hole-blocking/electron-transporting layer.338 For the device configuration ITO/263/BCP/LiF/Al, an average ηp of 8 lm W−1, at a brightness of 1065 cd m−2 (12 V) was obtained.338 The efficiencies of the two-layer devices are extraordinarily high at a useable brightness (17 cd A−1 and 16000 cd m−2) and are representative of those reported for more complex device architectures with high PLQYs.338 A high PLQY suggests that the dendrimer guest is evenly distributed throughout the emissive film.338 However, to realize highly efficient devices it is necessary to blend the dendrimers together with a suitable host.352 In order to examine the key role of the host, a first-generation light-emitting dendrimer (265) was designed.339 In this dendrimer identical building blocks were assembled into a macromolecular structure, significantly decreasing the interactions between the light-emitting Ir(ppy)3-cores.339 Studies of this system have shown that eliminating detrimental intermolecular interactions in the thin film does not compromise its charge transport properties.339 Highly branched dendrons have the potential to control intermolecular interactions and organize the surface groups, which play an important role in enhancing the physical properties of the dendrimer 267.341–343 The solution phase electrochemiluminescence of dendrimer 266 was found to be significantly improved when contained within the core of the dendrimer vs. the simple parent Ir(ppy)3 complex, despite the lower PLQY of the dendrimer.340 These properties are promising for the future applications of dendrimers as light-emitting materials in host-free devices. The presence of fluorene surface groups on dendrimer 268 enhance the solubility, as well as the efficiency of photoluminescence in the solid state.342 When fabricated into bilayer LEDs, dendrimer 267, without surface groups has a ηext of 10%, whereas ηext = 13% for dendrimer 268.342 The highly branched non-conjugated tetrahedral tetraphenylsilane dendrons around the Ir(ppy)3-core of dendrimer 269 also effectively inhibit intermolecular interactions between the core units in the films.343 In this respect, the dendritic structures provide a powerful and systematic way of controlling intermolecular interactions within the thin films, which decreases the rate of PL quenching.354 Studies on triplet–triplet annihilation were also carried out in neat films of electrophosphorescent dendrimers with [Ir(ppy)]-cores. The optical properties of phenylene- (263 and 264) and carbazole-based (270) dendrons have been studied by time-resolved photoluminescence.345 The photoluminescence emission for the Ir–carbazole complex 270 was slightly red-shifted at λmax ∼ 530 nm, when compared to dendrimers 263 and 264, due to its enhanced electron delocalization.345 The results reveal that the triplet–triplet annihilation constants varies in the range of 5–200 × 10−14 cm3 s−1, while the exciton diffusion lengths varies in the 2–10 nm, depending on the size of the dendrons.345 Furthermore, the emissive and charge transport properties of dendrimers 263 and 264 can be modified by the location and size of the dendritic arms. For this purpose, a novel para-appended first-generation Ir dendrimer 266 was studied.355 The emission of the dendrimer was red-shifted by 17 nm when compared to dendrimers 263 and 264 to give yellow-green emission with λmax = 535 nm, due to the extended conjugation of the ligand chromophore attached to the Ir(III) cation. Unfortunately, dendrimer 266 had a lower efficiency relative to the meta linked dendrimers 263 and 264, probably because of its lower PLQY.355 The same researchers have also developed a convergent synthetic strategy for the preparation of solution-processible green phosphorescent Ir(III) dendrimers with first- 270 and second-generation 271 dendrons, containing one and two carbazole units, respectively.344 Differential scanning calorimetry has shown that the glass-transition temperature (Tg) of dendrimer 270 was significantly higher than that of its first-generation, phenylene-based counterpart dendrimer 263, which is due to its higher molecular mass and the rigidity of its molecular structure. No Tg was detected within the limits of the experiment for dendrimer 271, due to its high molecular weight and large number of carbazole moieties. The PLQYs of neat films comprising of first- and second-generation dendrimers were significantly higher than those reported for phenylene-derived dendrimers.344 This improvement is ascribed to the increased steric demand of the carbazole unit relative to the phenyl ring, which reduces intermolecular interactions more effectively, causing the cores to be less emissive.344 Additionally, thin film hole mobilities of dendrimer 270 were found to be higher than those of its phenylene-based counterpart dendrimer 271.344 Two green and yellow-green multi-functional phosphorescent dendrimers 272 and 273 were also reported containing dendrons based on charge-transporting building blocks and a phosphorescent core.346 The use of non-conjugated alkyl chains to bridge the carbazole moieties increases the solubility of these compounds, preventing a decrease in their triplet energies. The current for a given voltage was higher in the OLED containing neat dendrimer 272versus OLEDs fabricated from Ir(ppy)3 core and phenylene-based dendrons, since the hole-transporting dendrons improve the hole drift mobility of the dendrimer. OLEDs fabricated with a blend film of dendrimer 272 together with the electron-transporting compound 1,3-bis[5-(4-tert-butylphenyl)-1,3,4-oxadiazole-2-yl]benzene (OXD-7) exhibit a high ηext of 7.6%, which was better than the maximum value of 5.2% for the device prepared
from dendrimer 273, most likely due to the location of the recombination site.346 Other studies concerned the synthesis and characterization of highly phosphorescent cyclometalated Ir(III) complexes with silane-based dendritic substituents (269 and 274–276).343,347–349 The tetrahedral configuration of the aryl substituents appended to the silicon atoms may provide a site-isolation effect if directly introduced as substituents onto the organic ligands of the Ir(III) complex. Furthermore, the highly efficient UV emission of aryl silanes suggests that they should have improved emission efficiencies when compared to their alkyl counterparts for OLED applications.356,357 As expected, studies confirm that the triphenylsilyl dendritic substituents play a key role in shielding the emissive Ir(ppy)3 core of the molecule from undesirable non-radiative pathways, without changing its emissive properties.347 Additionally, the introduction of triarylsilyl substituents affords high chemical stability and better solubilities that improves overall device performance.347 Despite these advantages, triarylsilyl groups also have major drawbacks that include low yields, difficult syntheses and low vapor pressures. In contrast, their trimethylsilyl counterparts are easily introduced into the ligand backbone that can be subsequently derivatized in high yields. In this respect, the combination of ppy together with trimethylsilyl substituents is a practical approach to achieve efficient phosphorescent emitters. In fact, studies reveal that the presence of trimethylsilyl functional groups confer very high thermal stabilities, good solubilities in common organic solvents, higher vapor pressures, and a higher volume of steric bulk.348 Ir(III) complexes containing trimethylsilyl groups also display yellow-green electroluminescence and significantly improved power efficiency when compared to Ir(ppy)3 devices, under the same conditions.348 Extending this concept, the introduction of bulky trimethylsilylxylene substituents onto the ppy ligand of the Ir-based complex 276 affords a device with ηext of 12.7%, which could be useful for the fabrication of white OLEDs for lighting applications.349 This improvement can at least in part be attributed to the increased steric bulk of the ligand, which hinders aggregation in the solid state at concentrations of 6% 276 doped into CBP.
Ir(ppy)3 derivatives as new types of phosphorescent materials containing hole-trapping diphenylamine (277), carbazole (278), phenoxazine (279), and phenothiazine (280) moieties, have also been synthesized in the search for effective methods for carrier injection into the phosphor of organic EL devices.350 Their PL maxima were found to be close to that of Ir(ppy)3, but slightly red and blue-shifted, depending on the electron donating/withdrawing nature of their respective substituents. In this respect, the presence of diphenylamine and phenoxazine groups increased the electron-donating ability of the Ir(ppy)3, while the carbazole unit caused a more unstable oxidation state. Among these complexes, EL devices containing complex 277 have shown a high EL performance with a maximum ηext of 12.2%,350 which is comparable to the values obtained with Ir(ppy)3.350 A representative example was also reported by Bolink et al., comprising of the novel cationic Ir(III) complex 281. By introducing positively charged, electron withdrawing side-groups onto the periphery of the ppy ligands,351 the emission of the complex was blue-shifted of ∼35 nm compared to Ir(ppy)3. Consequently, complex 281 emits bluish-green light, which is extremely appealing for future LED applications.
3 Pt(II) complexes containing 2-phenylpyrdiine ligands
Square-planar Pt(II) complexes have attracted much attention over recent years, due to their versatile spectroscopic properties. In contrast to Ir(III) complexes with octahedral geometries, square-planar Pt(II) complexes exhibit not only emission from ligand-centered (3LC) and/or 3MLCT states, but also tend to form stacked or dimeric structures, stabilized by Pt–Pt and/or π–π interactions that can be fairly problematic for their applications in real devices. A new approach for the fabrication of white OLEDS or WOLEDs, combines the monomer and excimer phosphorescence of the two emitters 282 and FIrpic, co-doped into a single emissive layer.358 This approach significantly simplifies the structure of the device without compromising efficiency.
