Continuing this study, efficient white electrophosphorescence was achieved by using the single emissive dopant, 283, which emits simultaneously from both monomer and aggregate states.359,360 Single dopant WOLEDs may solve the problems associated with differential dopant aging, while at the same time reducing the complexity of the device.359 In this respect, WOLEDs that utilize multiple dopants often change color over time due to differences in the degradation rates of each dopant.358 All dopant molecules are based on a series of P(II) β-diketonates.359 Increasing the degree of steric bulk of the Pt complex can cause a separation between the square-planar emissive molecules, as well as increase the solubility of the complexes, permitting their more uniform dispersal in the host matrix. For WOLEDs doped with complex 282, their EL spectra are dominated by an orange broad-band emission at λmax ∼ 590 nm, with minor peaks at ∼470 and ∼500 nm, attributed to the emission of the monomers.359 According to an extensive theoretical study,361 the Pt–Pt bimetallic interaction between adjacent molecules is a necessary condition to significantly shift the emission of these complexes to the red. As shown in Fig. 51, although all devices fabricated complex from 282 show both monomer and excimer emission, the relative intensities of the peaks vary.360 Moreover, in the absence of the Pt–Pt interactions, other types of intermolecular contacts e.g., Pt–π and π–π interactions, do not lead to such significant shifts in the PL spectra.362 Subsequent studies by Chen et al., describe the synthesis, characterization, and crystal structures of a family of new Pt(II) complexes, comprising of 3-aryl-substituted acac auxiliary ligands, 284–286.362 The PL spectrum of complex 286 possesses only a broad low-energy band at λmax = 605 nm, due to the presence of Pt–Pt interactions. In contrast, thin films prepared from the four complexes 282 and 284–286, display low-energy broad-band emissions of different wavelengths, depending on the fraction of Pt–Pt dimers that are present in the complexes. The ηext, as well as the current and power efficiency of non-doped devices based on these complexes decreases in the order 285 > 286 > 284 > 282. The higher efficiency of the non-doped device based on 285 is attributed to minor amounts of Pt–Pt dimers, that reduces self-quenching. Multi-functional Pt-derivatized random terpolymers assembled from monomers comprising of hole-transporting, electron-transporting, and emissive modules, 287–290, have also been prepared and investigated.363 These metallopolymers are stable in air, soluble in organic solvents and emit simultaneously in the blue and orange regions from both monomer and aggregate states.363 In addition to the intrinsic structural features of the polymer, the concentration of the Pt complex largely controls the extent of Pt–Pt interactions within the material.363 All devices fabricated from these polymers appear nearly white to the naked eye (pure white is x = 0.33, y = 0.33).363 Moreover, norbornene-derivatized platinum complexes, 291–294, comprising of 9,9-dialkyl-2,7-di-(carbazol-9-yl)fluorene monomers were synthesized and further copolymerized.364 Studies on these systems proved that excimer/aggregate emission from the platinum complexes are transferred to the EL spectra of light-emitting devices based on copolymers. Another interesting study was reported by Yang et al., using emissive platinum complexes containing carbazole moieties that are covalently attached to polyhedral oligomeric silsesquioxane (POSS) cores, 295–297.365 The POSS macromolecules containing chromophores and carrier-transporting moieties benefit from containing both small-molecule and polymer light-emitting materials in that they can be isolated in high purity and are solution processible. The aim of linking carbazole and platinum-complex moieties onto the same POSS core is to effectively dilute the concentration of the platinum complex within the single molecule, thereby adjusting the monomer and excimer/aggregate emission. Increasing the amount of carbazole moieties, as in complex 295vs. complex 296, improves the emission of the monomer in the solid state. OLEDs based on POSS materials displayed a maximum ηext value of ∼8%,365 which was significantly higher than that of similar devices containing a physical blend of the platinum complex together with a polymer matrix.365 The enhanced efficiency of POSS-based devices is ascribed to the reduced aggregation of the square planar Pt complexes and subsequent decrease in quenching at higher concentrations. Additionally, the ratio of monomer to excimer/aggregate emission intensity decreases as the concentration of the Pt complex in the POSS macromolecules increases, leading to variations in CIE coordinates from (0.46, 0.44) to (0.48, 0.48) to (0.53, 0.46), for devices containing complexes 296, 295, and 297, respectively. In a study published in 2005, the π–π stacking of mono-cyclometalated Pt(II) complexes 298–300 was prevented by increasing the steric bulk of the second auxiliary ligand in complexes bearing the same cyclometalated phenyl–pyridine ligand.366 This complexes are separated by intermolecular distances greater than 3.8 Å that does not completely eliminate interactions between neighboring Pt complexes in the solid-state, but does shift the emission wavelength of their solid-state electroluminescence spectra, to higher energies, i.e. λmax = 540 nm.366 In this respect, an efficient LED based on complex 299 displays green electroluminescence with an efficiency of 6 cd A−1.366 Other representative examples of this family of compounds are the trifunctional Pt(II) cyclometalated complexes proposed by Wong et al.367,368 In these systems, hole-transporting triarylamine, electron-transporting oxadiazole, and electroluminescent Pt(II) complexes are all combined within a single compound, 301–306.367,368 To understand the photophysics and electrochemistry of these multi-functional systems, their properties are compared to devices prepared from their constituent components. Hence devices prepared from complexes 305 and 306 were also studied, revealing that the Pt(II) chelates display good thermal (>250 °C under N2) and morphological stabilities. Furthermore, these complexes can also be vacuum-sublimed for applications as the emissive components of vapor-deposited electrophosphorescent OLEDs.367 The oxidation potentials of complexes 301–304 slightly decrease according to the following trend 301 > 302 > 303 > 304, which is attributed to the inductive effect of their R substituents.367
 Fig. 51  (a) Normalized spectra accompanied by CIE and CRI values of devices at 500 cd m−2 fabricated with complex 282. Device 3: ITO/PEDOT–PSS/NPD/Irppz/26mCPy–282/BCP/LiF/Al; device 4: ITO/PVK/NPD/Irppz/26mCPy–282/BCP/LiF/Al; device 5: ITO/PVK/26mCPy–282/BCP/LiF/Al; device 6: ITO/PEDOT–PSS/PVK/26mCPy–282/BCP/LiF/Al; (b) a photograph of a pixel with the structure of device 6. Reproduced from ref. 360 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2007. 
In addition to PL, electrofluorescence also depends on the electronic nature of the substituents, whereas electrophosphorescence energy is independent of R. Based on DFT studies, the HOMO of these complexes is located on the diarylamino groups, while the LUMO is localized on the π* orbitals of the oxadiazole heterocycle.368 Devices fabricated from complex 303 achieved excellent performances, with efficiencies up to 3.6%, 11.0 cd A−1, and 5.8 lm W−1.368 These results are presumably reached because of the better matching of the HOMO levels of their emissive complex with the NPB (hole-transport layer), which serves to afford an excellent balance between holes and electrons in the resulting device.368 Unfortunately, complexes 305 and 306 did not form good-quality films for devices fabrication. The physical and multi-functional properties of complex 301 also rendered it suitable for the fabrication of simple, small-molecule based device architectures.367,368 In this respect, the device ITO/CuPc/301/Ca/Al exhibited strong orange-yellow EL at λmax = 538 nm and 578 nm, with a low Von of 6 V. The device displays a maximum luminous efficiency of 1.2 cd A−1 and a luminance of 1065 cd m−2 at 14 V. The EL spectra of the OLED reveal no significant changes as the operating bias voltage was varied, a clear signature of a better performance compared to the more complex, multilayered device fabricated from 2-phenylpyridine.367,368 This study also highlights that strong interactions between emissive molecules appended to the triphenylamino moieties and the fluorene rings of 307 are very effective at overcoming triplet–triplet annihilation.369
In the following studies, a new series of Pt bearing triphenylene and carbazole substituents, 308–311, were designed and synthesized.370 To explore the structure–property relationships in these compounds, the linkers between the triphenylene and the Pt(II) moieties were varied from alkyl to aryl.370 Upon optical excitation, the excimer emission is effectively inhibited in these compounds due to the presence of peripheral side chains and additional carbazole units.370 Ho et al. reported a new series of Pt(II) cyclometalated complexes 312–321, in which electroluminescent Pt(II) complexes and hole-transporting carbazole substituents are integrated within a single molecule.371 The majority of these complexes were characterized by X-ray crystallography, demonstrating thermal stabilities with decomposition temperatures ranging from 234 to 350 °C.371 Based on electrochemical studies, it has been established that the addition of electron-withdrawing substituents such as a fluorine in complex 316, and a trifluoromethyl group in complex 315, stabilizes the LUMO levels, while electron-donating groups such as the methyl group in complex 317, destabilizes the LUMO level compared to the unsubstituted derivative (312).371 The energy of the LUMOs of these complexes therefore decreases in the order 217 > 312 > 316 > 315. The substituents on the phenyl ring of the carbazole moiety of these complexes had no effect on their redox properties. The excited-state properties of these complexes could be controlled by varying the electronic nature of the cyclometalate ligands. In this respect, functionalization of the organic ligands with the appropriate substituents allowed the emissive properties of the resulting compounds to be tuned from bright green, to yellow, orange and red light. Solution-processed electrophosphorescent OLEDs doped with phosphors based on complex 315 presented a maximum efficiency, ηext of 2.77%, corresponding to a power efficiency of 3.48 lm W−1 and a luminance of 8.49 cd A−1. The EL spectra of the device based on complex 315, slightly changes as a function of the concentration of the dopant due to excimer formation.371 More importantly, the emission caused by aggregation or π–π stacking was considerably weaker for devices containing complex 315, especially at high doping concentrations, when compared to the non-trifluoromethylated complexes 313 and 314. This is likely due to the presence of a bulky CF3 group, which alters the molecular packing and minimizes self-quenching behavior.371 Additionally, a pair of Pt(II) enantiomers 322, bearing sterically hindered chiral pinene substituents were also reported, displaying both lower self-quenching and triplet–triplet annihilation.372
The syntheses as well as the photo- and electroluminescence properties of novel Pt-containing copolymers prepared by the ring-opening metathesis polymerization (ROMP) of norbornene monomers were also investigated.383–386 This approach produced co-polymer materials that contain both hole transporting carbazole groups and emissive cyclometalated Pt(II) complexes that are anchored via a range of β-diketonates.373 Copolymers 323–325 were strongly emissive both in solution and the solid state, suggesting that the platinum complexes directly attached to a polymer chain are sufficiently isolated, preventing aggregate formation and excimer emission.373 Among these manufactured copolymers, the highest brightness was observed for a device based on 325, in which the cyclometalated Pt(II) complex is anchored by a pyrazolonate group.373
PhOLEDs with high ηext were demonstrated for the first time using the cyclometalated Pt(II) complex 326 bearing a bulky triarylboron group that prevented intermolecular π-to-π stacking and excimer formation.374 Moreover, the acetylacetonate ancillary ligand provides good solution and solid-state stabilities, possessing a high energy triplet state to ensure efficient phosphorescence from the boryl chromophore. Using this complex as the electrophosphor, a simplified green Pt(II)-based PhOLED, with a maximum ηext of 21% was obtained.374 Also, the orientation of the transition dipole moments of fluorescent molecules in OLEDs parallel to the substrate was simply enhances ηext by up to 45% compared with that containing randomly oriented molecules. Moreover, Pt(II) complexes were more suited to accepting horizontal orientation due to their square planar geometry.375 Hence, an OLED comprising of the linear-shaped Pt(II) complex 327 that adopts a horizontal orientation was synthesized displaying a maximum ηext of 15.8%.375 In order to suppress aggregation and excimer emission, the carbazole-modified green-emitting Pt(II) complex 328 was developed, containing a carbazole moiety, together with three hexadecyloxy methyl substituents on the parent ppy ligand.376 As previously mentioned, the carbazole unit is widely used as both a HT and emissive material in the field of OLEDs. Hexadecyloxy methyl groups were introduced to improve the dispersibility of this complex in the host matrix.376 Complex 328 displays good thermal stability and affords a PLQY that is three-time higher than its parent [(ppy)Pt(acac)] complex.376 Results from CV studies indicate that the incorporation of carbazole and hexadecyloxy methyl units into the ppy-based ligand, increases the energies of HOMO and LUMO frontier orbitals of the Pt(II) complex. At different dopant concentrations and applied voltages, aggregation and excimer emission were also effectively suppressed in devices doped with complex 328. In these devices, a maximum current efficiency of 1.51 cd A−1 was obtained, which is a 1.5 increase over [(ppy)Pt(acac)]. In addition to mononuclear complexes, numerous photoluminescence studies have also been carried out on multinuclear Pt(II) complexes.377,378,387–390 For example, Wang et al. probed the impact of molecular structure on the emissive properties of bi- and tri-nuclear organoplatinum complexes containing 2,2′-dipyridylamino ligands.387 In this respect, the dinuclear Pt(II) complex 329, comprising of a blend of monomolecular and excimer emission was prepared.377 OLED devices doped with complex 329 exhibited stable, white emission,377 whereas their (dfppy)Pt(pic)-doped mononuclear counterpart afforded orange-red emission.377 To further improve the solubility of dinuclear Pt(II) complexes and investigate the relationship between molecular structure and excimer emission,377 two dinuclear cyclometalated Pt(II) complexes (330 and 331), bearing long-dodecyloxy chains together with dipicolinic acid substituents were prepared and studied.378 In the presence of an applied electric field, doped single-emissive-layer PLEDs containing these Pt(II) complexes possess strong emission attributed to the single molecules rather than excimer emission, due to the presence of the two alkyloxy groups. Both dinuclear Pt(II) complexes exhibited intense UV-vis absorption and emission bands in solution. Improved electroluminescent emission with maximum current efficiencies of 1.3 cd A−1 and a maximum luminance of 1407 cd cm−2 was obtained for devices doped with complex 330.
Tsujimoto et al. developed a series of (CN)Pt(O,O′)-type complexes (332 and 333) that contain bdbp ancillary ligands, together with 2-(dibenzo[b,d]furan-4-yl)pyridinate (dbfp).391 These complexes were subsequently used for the fabrication of polymer-based LEDs that displayed a maximum ηext of 1.8% at 9.5 V and a brightness of 1845 cd m−2.392 To clarify the effect of the ancillary ligand on excimer formation in heteroleptic cyclometalated Pt(II) complexes, the same researchers investigated excimer-based PL and EL properties of the green-phosphorescent complexes 332 and 333, with bdbp and dpm (dipivaloylmethanate) ancillary ligands, respectively.379 In polymer thin films and PLEDs doped with complex 332, a red-shifted photoluminescence band at λmax > 600 nm, consistent with excimer emission was observed, together with bands at λmax = 516 and 552 nm, attributed to the emission of the monomer, which increased in intensity with the concentration of the dopant.379 Although films doped with complex 333 provided quite modest excimer-based photoluminescence, the relative intensity of the monomer emission was also much lower when compared to complex 332.379 This ancillary ligand effect is due to the considerable stacking-enhancing ability of bdbp. Normalized EL spectra and photographs of emitting PLEDs as the doping level of complex 332 is increased for devices A–D and the doping level of complex 333 is increased for devices A′–D′ are shown in Fig. 52. In this respect, as the doping levels in PLEDs containing complex 332 were increased from 7.1 to 24 wt%, the excimer-based EL shifts from green to orange, whereas for complex 333, the doping effect is much less remarkable, Fig. 52(b). Referring to CIE chromaticity coordinates, color tuning was possible for complex 332, with CIE values ranging from (0.40, 0.57) to (0.56, 0.43).379 It is also interesting to note that the efficient triplet excimer formation in PLEDs prepared from complex 332 originate from the direct charge recombination of anion and cation radicals that are generated by electron and hole injection.379 In order to develop systems that have more efficient monomer and excimer emission, a second family of dbfp-based cyclometalated Pt(II) complexes 334–339, were subsequently targeted.380 In later studies, electron donating and withdrawing substituents (F, CH3, and CF3) were introduced into the dbfp cyclometalated ligand framework to study their effects on the quantum yields and colors of the resulting complexes.380 These studies reveal that substitution at the 4-position of the dbfp ligand significantly improves the emission efficiencies of this family of Pt(II) complexes in polymer media.380 Furthermore, PLEDs doped with the methyl-substituted complex had relatively high device efficiencies. Among these complexes, complex 335 was sufficiently soluble in organic solvents to be fabricated into PLEDs at high doping levels.
 Fig. 52  Normalized EL spectra of PLEDs: (a) devices A–D and (b) devices A′–D′ at different doping levels of complexes 332 and 333, respectively. (c) Photographs of devices A–D and devices A′–D′ upon application of a voltage. Reproduced from ref. 379 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2014. 
In order to improve the thermal stability and performance of the electrophosphorescent devices, two functionalized Pt(II)-based triplet emitters (340 and 341) were later developed with a flexible phenoxy group on the ppy ligand, which improves the morphology of the emissive layer due to the flexible nature of the oxygen linker.381 Because of their special excimer emission, a wide range of colors from cyan to orange were observed as the concentration of the Pt(II) complex is increased.
A series of Pt(II) complexes, 342–345, with a tpip ancillary ligand were also synthesized to study the substitution effects of CF3 groups.382 From this work,382 it is apparent that a CF3 group on C3 of the ligand causes a hypsochromic shift in the emission of complex 343, whereas a CF3 groups on C2 and C4 leads to a bathochromic shift in complexes 342 and 344, respectively. Among these complexes, complex 343 displays a maximum luminance of over 39000 cd m−2, accompanied by current and power efficiencies of 41.0 cd A−1 and 31.0 lm W−1, respectively. Considering all of the devices prepared and studied to-date it is apparent that the best OLEDs are based on rare metal complexes containing ppy and polypyridyl ligands, with EQE values >10%, and are typically green emitters, Fig. 53. In this respect, complex 154 has the highest EQE value reported to-date of more than 25%.
 Fig. 53  Plot of EQE vs. wavelength for second generation OLEDS containing ppy and polypyridyl ancillary ligands with EQE's greater than >10%. The numbers refer to the numbering scheme for the respective complexes in the sections of this review. 
4 Third-generation OLEDs: TADFs
4.1 Introduction: TADF based on Cu(I) complexes
Cu(I) complexes containing polypyridyl ligands have been met with great interest since they are relatively inexpensive to prepare and comprise of abundant first row transition metal ions, which makes them attractive replacements to the rare metal polypyridyl complexes previously described.88,178,393,394 In early studies, Buckner and McMillin observed that Cu(I) polypyridyl complexes display metal to ligand charge transfer (MLCT) based luminescence due to the d10 electronic configuration of the Cu(I) ion, providing high PLQY in both solution and solid films.395–399 Over the past decade studies have revealed that most TADF Cu(I) metal complexes, which show promise for OLED applications, contain polypyridyl ligands.400–409 In fact, in analogy with the Ir-based systems, the use of polypyridyl ligands provides a strategy to optimize the emission properties of OLEDs, since emission often arises from the MLCT state.410 To the best of our knowledge, the EQEs of OLEDs based on Cu(I) TADFs are now exceeding 23%, which is comparable to the efficiency of OLEDs based on the Ir(III) emitters, indicating a bright future for this new generation of OLEDs.411 Despite this promise, the widely-recognized disadvantage of Cu(I) polypyridyl complexes are their long lifetimes, that are typically in the range of several hundred microseconds,412 attributed to weak spin–orbit coupling (SOC), resulting in low radiative rates for the T1 → S0 transitions.402,413 Nevertheless, in reverse intersystem crossing (RISC), low SOC conditions are possible, with the population of T1 that may be thermally transferred to the S1 excitons for light generation. The small energy separation between lowest excited singlet (S1) and triplet states (T1) in Cu(I) complexes may therefore permit the thermal activation of excitons from T1 to S1 and then the subsequent transfer of energy from the S1 → S0 transition becomes effectively a radiative channel for light emission. In reality, the low SOC in Cu(I) polypyridine complexes is now more commonly referred to as TADF.414–416 In fact, TADF is now considered to be a major breakthrough for OLED technology, which makes these compounds very promising for the development of highly efficient third generation OLEDs.404,417,418 As a consequence, many organic and non-metal TADF compounds have been reported in recent years, which have been the subject of several reviews, and thus are not within the scope of this article.112,113,419–422 In contrast, however, the progress of TADF-OLEDs based on metal–polypyridyl complexes has not been intensively reviewed to-date and thus is the focus of the next section of this review paper. Considering the importance of blue emission in the field of OLEDs, the synthesis and characterization of a series of cationic cuprous complexes with green-blue to blue emission comprised of (POP = bis[2-(diphenylphosphino)phenyl]ether) and N-linked 2-pyridyl pyrazolate diimine ligands, [Cu(pypz)(POP)](BF4) (346), [Cu(pympz)(POP)](BF4) (347), and [Cu(pytfmpz)(POP)](BF4) (348)408 (where pypz = 1-(2-pyridyl)pyrazole; pympz = 3-methyl-1-(2-pyridyl)pyrazole, and pytfmpz = 3-trifluoromethyl-1-(2-pyridyl)pyrazole) has been reported.329 It now well established that the best strategy to obtain blue emission is to chemically tune the HOMO–LUMO energy gap423 of the coordination complexes. In this respect, pypz ligands are selected for their electron-donating properties which destabilize the LUMO energy levels of luminescent dopants, thereby increasing the HOMO–LUMO energy gaps, and therefore shifting the emission of their resulting complexes to the blue.329 In addition, it is worth noting that various substituents on the pyrazole rings of pypz ligands can be introduced to modulate their emission efficiencies and colors. Another remarkable phenomenon found in these complexes is the correlation of the emission decay time with the spectroscopic properties as a function of the temperature, due to the relatively small energy gap between their singlet and triplet states.329 In this way, low-temperature phosphorescence (LTP) and high-temperature thermally activated delayed fluorescence (HT-TADF).329 The two phenomena are present with emission decay times that are dramatically reduced from hundreds to about ten microseconds as the temperature is increased.329 When these complexes are used as dopants in different hosts for the fabrication of solution-processed OLEDs, efficiencies up to 23.68 cd A−1 and a peak brightness of 2033 cd cm−2 were obtained for complex 348 doped into 2,6-bis(N-carbazolyl)pyridine (2,6-mCPy).329 This high value is attributed to the “adequate” confinement of the triplet excitons and charge carriers.329 However, the low efficiencies of the OLEDs based on complexes 346 and 347 are attributed to the approximately equal LUMO energy levels of the host mCPy and the 346 or 347 dopants.329 In order to modify TADF in Cu(I) complexes, a NHetPHOS complex containing a butterfly-shaped Cu2I2 unit coordinated by two ancillary phosphine ligands and bridged by an N,P-ligand, complex 349, was reported.411 This compound is air stable and has a lower triplet energies ΔE(T1–S0) of up to 200 meV compared to other green-emitting Cu(I) compounds.396 Another advantage of this binuclear Cu-complex is its high thermal stability (Tdecomp = 290 °C) due the four-fold bridging of its Cu(I) centers. This multi-bridged binuclear Cu(I) complex lends itself well to OLED applications because of its well-defined structure, good processability, high PLQY in PYD2-doped films and low triplet energy, due to the singlet harvesting mechanism.411 In fact, for OLEDs containing complex 349, a high EQE value of 23% was obtained, which is the highest value reported to date for OLEDs fabricated from either vacuum or solution processed emissive layers of Cu(I) complexes. More importantly, this value is comparable to best efficiencies reported for state-of-the-art vacuum sublimed devices based on Ir(III) emitters. The former device shows a pronounced roll-off of the current efficiency with increasing luminance due to a loss of charge balance, most likely since most of the hole transport does not occur via the host material, but via the emitter itself. Nevertheless, it is apparent that this multi-bridged binuclear Cu(I) complex paves the way for the future development of new families of very efficient OLEDs.
In parallel studies, two blue-green Cu(I) complexes [Cu(PNNA)(POP)](BF4) (350) and [Cu(PNNA)(Xantphos)](BF4) (351) were prepared comprising of a functional diimine and the phosphine ligands, PNNA = 9,9-dimethyl-10-(6-(3-phenyl-1H-pyrazol-1-yl)pyridin-3-yl)-9,10-dihydroacridine, and POP = bis[2-(dipenylphosphino)phenyl]ether, Xantphos = 4,5-bis-(diphenylphosphino)-9,9-dimethylxanthene.424 The luminescence properties of these complexes are attributed to two inter-convertible excited states that are in thermal equilibrium, i.e., S1 and T1 at 293 and 77 K respectively. It is interesting to note that increasing the steric hindrance of the ligands increases the PLQYs. In these cuprous complexes it is noteworthy that a 9,9-dimethylacridan donor substituent was subsequently appended to their diimine ligands due to superior hole-transport ability. In most cuprous complexes, luminescence is caused by a metal to ligand charge transfer (MLCT) transition, but in complexes containing a functional dimethylacridine moiety an ILCT-based luminescence is observed. Finally, the highest efficiency, brightness and best blue electroluminescence was observed for a device with complex 351 as the dopant, which is attributed to the presence of its bulky, rigid ligands.
In 2017, Ling and co-workers began systematic studies on three green emissive binuclear cuprous complexes.176 In these complexes tetraimine derivatives together with the bisphosphine ligands (pytzph = 6,6′-(1-phenyl-1,2,4-triazole-3,5-diyl)bis(2-methylpyridine), pytzphcf = 6,6′-(1-(4-(trifluoromethyl)phenyl)-1,2,4-triazole-3,5-diyl)bis-(2-methylpyridine), pytzphcz = 9-(4-(3,5-bis(6-methylpyridin-2-yl)-1,2,4-triazol-1-yl)phenyl)-carbazole and POP = bis[2-(diphenylphosphine)phenyl]ether), were used to prepare the corresponding cationic cuprous complexes, [Cu2(pytzph)(POP)2](BF4)2 (352), [Cu2(pytzphcf)(POP)2](BF4)2 (353) and [Cu2(pytzphcz)(POP)2](BF4)2 (354).176 It is notable that the contributions to the emission from the S1 and T1 states differ as a function of the temperature. In this respect, below 90 K, the intensity contribution from the phosphorescence increases but as the temperature increases, the emission from the S1 state via TAD increases in intensity. At room temperature in the solid state, these complexes exhibit highly efficient green TADF due to the small activation energy ΔEST, which causes the lower energy T1 excitons to convert gradually into the S1 state through reverse intersystem crossing by thermal activation, decreasing the radiative lifetime. Complex 354 containing a carbazole moiety shows the highest device efficiency for the solution-processed OLEDs. This behavior can be attributed to its high PLQY and the excellent hole transporting abilities of the carbazole group, whereas the presence of the electron-withdrawing CF3 group in complex 353 results in a poorer quantum efficiency. Given that sterically bulky carborane containing ligands display superior properties such as (i) thermal stability,428 (ii) unique electronic effects, (iii) ability to tune the HOMO and LUMO (iv) intermolecular interactions, (v) high photoluminescent PLQY,429–432 (vi) hydrophilicity433–437 and (vii) concentration quenching behavior,438–440 Gary et al. proposed three cationic Cu(I) complexes, 355–357, bearing dppnc (7,8-bis(diphenylphosphino)-7,8-dicarba-nido-undecaborate) and neocuproine ligands. Studies of these systems reveal that by modifying the structure of the neuquoperine, the emission color could be adjusted from green to deep red.425 The unique molecular and electronic structures of these complexes are understood and have been correlated to their photo-physical properties by DFT calculations.425 The most important conclusion from this study is that the efficiency and emission color of cuprous complexes can be strongly influenced by both T1 energy levels of the host material. Solution-processed OLEDs were fabricated from the aforementioned cuprous complexes and all show the TADF mechanism.425 Maximum EQEs of 18.46, 14.30, and 10.17% were obtained in the green, yellow and red region for Cu(dppnc)-G (355), Cu(dppnc)-Y (356) and Cu(dppnc)-R (357), respectively, see Fig. 54. Furthermore, the performance of these devices with dopant concentrations ranging from 4 to 20 wt% were recorded and are summarized in Table 9. Pure red emission with an EQE of 10.17%, was achieved for the device comprising of 4 wt% of the Cu(dppnc)-R emitter doped into a CBP host, which represents the highest efficiency reported for a red Cu-OLED to date.441–444 The first examples of TADF-OLEDs based on lanthanide polypyridyl complexes were prepared to overcome the difficulties associated with the development of red OLEDs.
 Fig. 54  Molecular structure of cuprous complexes containing bulky carborane phosphine ligands including Cu(dppnc) (355), affording green emission, Cu(dppnc) (356), affording yellow emission and Cu(dppnc) (357), affording red emission. Reproduced from ref. 425 with permission from the Wiley-VCH, copyright 2017. 
In this respect, the europium complex, tris(dibenzoylmethane)mono(1,10-phenanthroline)Eu(III), (Eu(DBM)3Phen), 358, was prepared.426 This complex afforded red OLEDs with narrow, sharp emissions attributed to 5D0 → 7F2 transitions of the 4f ion.367 These complexes are characterized as TADF OLEDs due to the small energy gap (ΔEST) between the S1 and T1 states that facilitates efficient triplet reverse intersystem crossing (RISC) and reduces non-radiative decay. It is worth noting that in complex 338, the 4f ion is eight-coordinate that could prevent quenching of the luminescence. Furthermore, the energy transfer to the metal centre is achieved via both the DBM and phen ligands that effectively improves the injection and transport properties of the carriers and shields the 4f ion from quenching.367 A maximum current efficiency of 5.51 cd A−1, power efficiency of 4.94 lm W−1, EQE of 3.11% and a low turn-on voltage of 2.5 V, was achieved for the TADF exciplex host m-bis(N-carbazolyl)benzene PO-T2T (mCP:PO-T2T), doped with 10 wt% complex 338.367 Furthermore, the performance of these devices with dopant concentrations ranging from 4 to 20 wt% were recorded and are summarized in Table 9. Pure red emission with an EQE of 10.17%, was achieved for the device comprising of 4 wt% of the Cu(dppnc)-R emitter doped into a CBP host, which represents the highest efficiency reported for a red Cu-OLED to date.441–444 The first examples of TADF-OLEDs based on lanthanide polypyridyl complexes were prepared to overcome the difficulties associated with the development of red OLED.426 The low turn-on voltage of the device can be ascribed to the barrier-free carrier injection, but the high roll-off ratio (42%) is attributed to the lack of balance between the transport and recombination of the carriers. The maximum energy transfer efficiency between the exciplex host and the Eu-complex takes place for an optimal doping concentration of 10 wt%.445,446 This result highlights the potential of lanthanide complexes possessing near-IR emission to find applications as new advanced functional electroluminescence materials.445,446 The advantage of using metal-based complexes that undergo TADF over organic compounds is attributed to their improved thermal stabilities due to their heavier molecular weights, increasing the rates of intersystem crossing (KISC),447,448 as well as the significant reduction in ΔEST and increase in kRISC by changing the dihedral angles between the acceptor and the donor. In order to overcome the low thermal stability of the copper complex, Sakai et al. reported the first zinc complexes namely, bis[2-(benzo[d]oxazol-2-yl)-5-(10H-phenoxazin-10-yl)phenolato]zinc(II) and bis[2-(benzo[d]oxazol-2-yl)-4-(10H phenoxazin-10-yl)phenol]zinc(II), 359 and 360, in which thermally activated delayed fluorescence arises from intra-ligand charge transfer.427 The thermal stability is the key feature of these zinc complexes, making them suitable for fabrication into OLEDs by vacuum deposition methods.427 The large kRISC and small ΔEST in complex 359 improves the efficiency of up conversion and relaxing exciton annihilation.427 Replacing Zn with Li (361) or Mg (362) affords devices displaying reasonable parameters that are summarized in Table 9. The PL decay lifetimes of these complexes follow the trend 361 > 362 > 359 and analyzing the kinetic rates, a systematic atomic-number effect was identified.427 To summarize, in typical metal complexes comprising of conventional fluorescent materials, spin orbit coupling promotes KISC with increasing atomic number that eventually leads to a decrease in the PLQY. However, in the aforementioned TADF complexes, the kRISC values were enhanced, while the ΦPL remains constant.427 Comparing the efficiencies of the four devices it is apparent that complex 359 has the largest ηext which is close to 20%.
4.2 Promising Cu(I) and Ag(I) complexes as TADF emitters
Although there are many reports concerning new efficient complexes for potential applications as TADF emitters, only a few examples have actually been fabricated into OLED or LECs devices. Therefore, in this final section the focus is on TADF Cu(I) complexes with emissive properties that are particularly attractive for lighting applications. The first of these complexes is the highly luminescent Cu(I), [Cu(dmp)(phanephos)]+ (363), with two chelating, dmp = 2,9-dimethyl-1,10-phenanthroline and phanephos = 4,12-bis(diphenylphosphino)-[2.2]paracyclophane ligands.449 For this complex at T < 120 K, a long-lived phosphorescence of 240 μs was observed as shown in Fig. 55. In contrast, at ambient-temperature, TADF emission is observed and the decay time becomes shorter, dropping to 14 μs. Moreover, as the temperature increases, kr increases by a factor of twenty i.e. from kr = 3 × 103 s−1, to kr (DF) = 6 × 104 s−1. It is noteworthy that the phanephos and dmp ligands confer a rigid structural topology of the Cu(I) complex and, as a result, non-radiative relaxation processes to the ground state are likely suppressed, so an intense green-yellow luminescence is observed even in solution. The authors highlight the potential of this rigid Cu(I) complex for used in efficient OLEDs due to its small ΔE(S1–T1) energy separation as a result of TADF. The same researchers, also published studies on the very intense green-yellow luminescent mononuclear Cu(I) complex Cu(dppb)-(pz2Bph2) (364), together with two other compounds Cu(pop)(pz2Bph2) (365), and [Cu(dmp)(phanephos)]+ (363) where dppb = 1,2-bis-(diphenylphosphino)benzene and pz2Bph2 = diphenylbis-(pyrazol-1-yl)borate, which exhibit TADF at ambient temperatures.450
 Fig. 55  Emission decay time vs. temperature for microcrystalline [Cu(dmp)(phanephos)](PF6), 363, excited at λexc = 355 nm (pulse width 7 ns). The emission was detected at λdet = 550 nm. The solid line represents a fit of bi-exponential decay model to the experimental data with a phosphorescence decay time τ(T1) = 240 μs, at 77 K. The resulting fit parameters are ΔE(S1–T1) = 1000 cm−1 and τ(S1) = 40 ns, representing the magnitude of the singlet–triplet splitting and the decay time of the spontaneous S1 → S0 fluorescence, respectively. The spontaneous fluorescence is not observed directly due to much faster intersystem crossing. τ(DF) = 14 μs which is the decay time of the delayed fluorescence at ambient temperature. Reproduced from ref. 449 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2013. 
For all the three Cu(I) complexes, the energy separation ΔE(S1–T1) lies within the range 370–1000 cm−1, affording very different emission colors. The luminescence properties of these compounds between 1.5 and 300 K are presented in Fig. 56. The three compounds exhibit emission quantum yields higher than 70%, hence it is very likely that these materials could be exploited for the fabrication of highly efficient OLEDs. Interestingly, the smallest energy separation ΔE(S1–T1) = 370 cm−1 (46 eV), between the first excited singlet and triplet states was found for complex 364 that also has the shortest TADF decay time of any Cu(I) complex reported to-date, i.e. τ (TADF, 300 K) = 3.3 μs. The small energy separation ΔE(S1–T1) in these complexes is attributed to the distinct CT character of the emissive states due to the small quantum-mechanical exchange interaction.
 Fig. 56  Luminescence decay times vs. temperature for microcrystalline samples of complexes 364 (green triangles), 365 (blue circles) and 363 (black squares). The dominant emission mechanisms at 30, 80, and 300 K are indicated. Reproduced from ref. 450 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2015. 
In a notable effort to explore the relationship between the position and the type of functional substituents on the organic ligands vs. the optical performances of the resulting complexes, a new series of cuprous complexes, 366–370, were prepared with POP (bis[2-(dipenylphosphino)-phenyl]ether) and 6-methyl-2-(1-(R-phenyl)-1H-pyrazol-3-yl)pyridine ligands in which the substituent R = H for complex 366, 2-CH3 for complex 367, 4-CH3 for complex 368, 2-CF3 for complex 369 and 4-CF3 for complex 370.451 These complexes show prominent blue to blue-green TADF with quantum yields close to 100% in the solid state and are air stabile with no visible decomposition up to 300 °C under nitrogen.451 As expected, the results of this study indicate that charge, steric hindrance and the electron withdrawing/accepting nature of the substituents on the organic ligands significantly affect the optical properties complexes.451 It should also be noted that only small variations (∼16 nm) in the emission spectra were recorded for single crystals vs. thin films and that these complexes are significantly stable compared to processing, as shown in Fig. 57. Bearing these factors in mind, we therefore conclude that this family of Cu(I) complexes are also very promising emissive materials for the fabrication of stable, blue OLEDs.
 Fig. 57  Photographic images (a) and emission curves (b) for a microcrystalline powder, thin film and solvent samples of complex 368. Reproduced from ref. 451 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2015. 
In two important studies published in 2017,452,453 a team of researchers led by Shafikov and Yersin studied the effects of substituents452 on the photophysical properties as well as improving the TADF efficiency of a series of new Ag(I) complexes containing a phenanthroline core and negatively charged electron donor (P2-nCB) ligands namely, Ag(phen)(P2-nCB) (371), Ag(idmp)(P2-nCB) (372), Ag(dmp)(P2-nCB) (373) and Ag(dbp)(P2-nCB) (374), in which P2-nCB = bis(diphenylphosphine)-nido-carborane, phen = 1,10-phenanthroline, idmp = 4,7-dimethyl-1,10-phenanthroline, dmp = 2,9-dimethyl-1,10-phenanthroline, and dbp = 2,9-di-n-butyl-1,10-phenanthroline (Fig. 58). Ag(I) complexes generally exhibit long lived phosphorescence from ligand-centered (LC) excited states of 3π–π* character.454–458
 Fig. 58  From left to right, molecular structures of the Ag(I) complexes 371, 373 and 374. Reproduced from ref. 452 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. 
For TADF, the d orbitals of these complexes need to be destabilized in order to have significant contributions from their HOMO, HOMO−1, and HOMO−2 frontier orbitals.
In this respect, the use of a strongly electron donating ligand can address this challenge, especially if the ligand is negatively charged. In this study, 1,10-phenanthroline was coupled with a phosphine ligand to stabilize the orbitals (LUMO, LUMO+1) of the complexes whose ΦPL values follow the order Ag(phen)(P2-nCB) (371) < Ag(idmp)(P2-nCB) (372) < Ag(dmp)(P2-nCB) (373) < Ag(dbp)-(P2-nCB) (374). This trend reflects the increasing rigidity of the complexes that in turn increases the radiative decay and decreases the non-radiative decay rates.
One of the important parameters for TADF efficiency that is proportional to the radiative rate is the oscillator strength of the S1 ↔ S0 transition f (S1 ↔ S0), which also follows the above trend due to its dependence on the coordination geometry of the complex in the emitting state. Furthermore, the oscillator strength for the S0 → S1 transition in these complexes is an order of magnitude higher when compared to other organo-metallic TADF materials with similar ΔE(S1–T1) splitting, In fact, the large, allowed S0 ↔ S1 transition for Ag(dbp)(P2-nCB) (374), results in extraordinary emission properties due to the fact that it exhibits ground breaking TADF efficiency, including a high TADF rate with a significantly short radiative TADF decay time of only τr = 1.4 μs. The ΦPL reported for a powder sample of complex 374 at ambient temperature reached to 100% (Fig. 59 and 60).452,453 As a result, these compounds are very exciting for applications in lighting technology. In fact, Ag(dbp)(P2-nCB) (374) is the first compound ever reported with a TADF decay time that is shorter than the phosphorescence decay time τr of 1.5 μs reported for the benchmark Ir(ppy)3.458
 Fig. 59  Photophysical properties of Ag(dbp)(P2-nCB) (374) as a powder sample shown on a simplified energy diagram. N.B. Up-ISC is also referred to as reverse ISC (RISC). Reproduced from ref. 453 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. 
 Fig. 60  Top: Luminescence decay times (τ) of Ag(dbp)(P2-nCB) (374) for a powder measured at different temperatures; bottom: the luminescence decay times (τ) plotted vs. temperature. The values of k(S1 → S0) = 5.6 × 107 s−1 (18 ns) and ΔE(S1–T1) = 650 cm−1 result from a fit of bi-exponential decay model to the experimental τ(T) values, with τ(T1) fixed to 1570 μs as determined directly for T < 60 K (plateau). Reproduced from ref. 453 with permission from the American Chemical Society, copyright 2017. 
Finally, this study would not be complete without highlighting the versatility of tetrazole as an ancillary ligand. In this respect, tetrazole is well known to chemists since it has been previously used for the preparation of a broad range materials that include biological compounds,459,460 as well as in the photovoltaics461 and optoelectronics,462 including OLEDs, applications.297
However, to the best of our knowledge, there are no reports concerning the use of this ligand for the discovery of TADF complexes. One of the most important goals in developing new TADF emitters, is to determine the rate at which singlet states of the compounds convert to triplets. In 2016 Bergmann et al., reported the first calculation of the rate of intersystem crossing for a of Cu(I) tetrazolate complex. Later, picosecond time-resolved solid state photoluminescence was reported for the highly emissive mononuclear TADF Cu(I) emitter, [(bis(2(diphenylphosphino)phenyl)ether)Cu(5-(2-pyridyl)tetrazolate)] (375).175 An ISC rate constant of 3.7 × 1010 s−1 with an ISC time constant of 27 ps, together with slow reverse intersystem crossing and a TADF average lifetime of 11.5 ms was also reported for this complex. Information concerning intersystem crossing has shed important light on excited-state mechanisms in Cu(I) TADFs containing tetrazole ligands, whose emissive properties are very promising for OLED applications.
Although, the concept of TADF based OLEDs is a relatively new phenomenon, the first families of these compounds look very promising and reflect the outstanding achievements accomplished in this field to-date. By harvesting both singlet and triplet excitons for light emission, OLEDs with performances that exceed their phosphorescence-based counterparts can now be fabricated. To summarize, TADF emitters have the high efficiencies associated with second generation (triplet) emitters and high stabilities that are comparable with first generation of materials, while overcoming triplet–triplet annihilation and the low device stabilities commonly encountered in phosphorescent OLEDs. There is no doubt that new discoveries will continue to inspire future generations of scientists and provide a solid foundation for the development of diverse families of efficient TADF emitters in the coming years.
5 Conclusions
The main objective of this review has been to provide a comprehensive, authoritative and critical reference for scholars working in the interdisciplinary field of light-to-energy conversion. Research progress over the past two decades concerning the development of metal complexes containing polypyridyl ligands is highlighted. Given the growing demand for new materials with enhanced quantum yields and high luminescence efficiencies, attention was focused on the highly efficient, emissive complexes whose ancillary ligands have been chemically tuned to encompass the entire visible region. The versatility of these compounds to provide light-emission management solutions for multi-color displays, as well as advanced lighting applications are also highlighted. Current challenges in the field of molecular electronics are presented together with strategies to overcome these obstacles, so that the true commercial promise of these compounds can be realized. Significant progress towards the development of second and third generation TADF-OLEDs based on organometallic and metal–organic complexes whose ancillary ligands can be systematically tuned has been discussed. Another objective of this work was the analysis of the state of the art and how to bridge the gap between molecular design and commercialization, which is currently one of the “hottest” challenges in this field of science to date. In this view, by carefully reviewing all the families of polypyridyl complexes reported to-date we conclude that for the development of efficient Ir(III) based OLEDs, the most promising complex is 172, which comprises two ppy ligands with fluorine substituents on the 3,4,6 positions of the phenyl rings, together with a bidentate polypyridyl ligand containing a tetraphenylimidodiphosphinate (tpip) moiety. In contrast for LECs, compound 120 containing a bpy ancillary ligand is one of the most outstanding Ir cyclometalated complexes reported to-date with an EQE of 14.9% and near-quantitative internal quantum efficiency. However, more work is needed to increase the device stability, keeping this high device efficiency. As to date, LECs are still facing the challenge to meet high efficiencies, stabilities, and color control by the same type of emitter. Finally, one of the most efficient TADF inorganic complexes containing a polypyridyl ligand with an EQE of over 23% is NHetPHOS (349), comprising of a butterfly-shaped Cu2I2 unit coordinated by two ancillary phosphine ligands that are bridged by an N,P-ligand. This compound is particularly promising for future commercialization due to its high thermal stability attributed to the presence of the multi-bridged binuclear Cu(I) ion.
To conclude we believe that future studies on high-performance OLEDs will not only continue to advance their commercial applications but will also accelerate the discovery of new fundamental science. If chemists continue to successfully work alongside scientists from neighboring fields, many new, exciting advances in this field will be possible.

Table Content:

From left to right: Soheila Karimi, Hashem Shahroosvand and Babak Pashaei
	Soheila Karimi (left) completed both her BSc (2014) and MSc (2016) studies in Chemistry at the University of Zanjan (ZNU). She is currently a PhD student at the ZNU, under the supervision of Prof. Hashem Shahroosvand. Her PhD studies will be on “low-cost perovskite solar cells and light-emitting diodes based on novel polypyridyl compounds”. Babak Pashaei (right) obtained his PhD degree in Inorganic Chemistry from the ZNU in 2018. From 2019 to now, he is a Postdoc research associate with Prof. Hashem Shahroosvand at the ZNU. His research interests are mainly focused on the design and synthesis of novel hole transport materials in perovskite solar cells (PSCs) and ionic transition-metal complexes for flexible light emitting electrochemical cells (LECs). (Middle) Hashem Shahroosvand earned his PhD degree (2008) in Inorganic Chemistry at University of Sistan & Baluchetsan, IRAN, under the supervision of Prof. Mozhgan Khorasani-Motlagh. He then became a postdoctoral fellow in the group of Prof. Nasser Safari and Prof. Ezeddin Mohajerani at laser & plasma research institute, Shahid Beheshti University, Iran. From 2010, he joined the faculty of Chemistry of the ZNU as an associate professor. His research encompasses molecularly engineered emitters for LEC application and HTM for PSC.

Parisa Abbasi
	Parisa Abbasi is currently a PhD student in Inorganic Chemistry with Prof. M. Pilkington at Brock University (Ontario, Canada). She received her MSc from ZNU (Iran) in 2013 under the supervision of Prof. H. Shahroosvand, working on the synthesis and study of solar cells. She is currently the recipient of an Ontario Trillium Fellowship and her research interests lie in the synthesis and study of single molecule magnets, as well as hole transport materials for solar cells.

Melanie Pilkington
	Melanie Pilkington is an Associate Professor of Inorganic Chemistry at Brock University in Ontario, Canada. She studied for both her BSc and PhD degrees at the University of Kent (UK), completing her PhD with Prof. J. D. Wallis in 1995. Following Postdoctoral studies with Professors Hans-Beat Bürgi (Berne, Switzerland), A. K. Kirby (Cambridge, U.K.) and Silvio Decurtins (Berne, Switzerland), she started her independent academic career as a Tier II Canada Research Chair in Novel Hybrid Materials at Brock University in 2004. She was the recipient of an SNF-Förderungsprofessuren Fellowship, as well as an early career Premier's Research Excellence Award. Her research interests include the synthesis and study of molecule-based magnetic, optical and electronic materials.

Antonino Bartolotta
	Antonino Bartolotta gained his degree in Chemistry from the University of Messina in Italy. Since 1984 he has been researcher at the Institute for the Chemical Physical processes of the Italian National Research Council. His scientific activity started in the field of condensed matter Physics, mainly devoting to the glass transition and the dynamical processes in disordered systems (glasses, polymer and vitreous ionic conductors). His current research interest is focused in the field of nanomaterials and device for energy conversion and storage, including dye sensitized solar cells (DSSCs) based on synthetic and natural dyes, carbon nanostructured materials (graphene and related 2D materials) for advanced optoelectronic and photovoltaic applications. He has been involved in many research projects and he is author/coauthor of more than 100 scientific publications in International peer-reviewed journals, book chapters and speaker in International congress.

From left to right: Julio Fernandez-Cestau, Elisa Fresta, and Rubén D. Costa
	Julio Fernandez-Cestau – MSc (2005) in Chemistry and PhD in Chemistry (2011) from the U. de La Rioja (Spain) – is a Marie Curie postdoctoral fellow at IMDEA Materials Institute. His research covers the use of organometallic complexes for catalysis, photonics, and medical applications, with special emphasis of the design of emissive complexes for lightning devices. Elisa Fresta – BSc (2014) in Chemistry and MSc (2016) in Industrial Chemistry from the U. of Turin (Italy) – is a PhD candidate at the IMDEA Materials Institute (Spain). She is working on lighting technologies with a focus on hybrid light-emitting diodes and light-emitting electrochemical cells. Rubén D. Costa – MSc in Chemistry (2006) and PhD in Chemistry (2010) from the U. of Valencia (Spain) – is the head of the Hybrid Optoelectronic Materials and Devices Lab at IMDEA Materials Institute. His research involves the development of new electroluminescent materials, nanocarbon-based hybrids, and bio-hybrid materials for lighting, photovoltaic, and sensing applications.

Francesco Bonaccorso
	Francesco Bonaccorso gained a PhD from the University of Messina after working at the Italian National Research Council, the University of Cambridge and the University of Vanderbilt. In June 2009 he was awarded a Royal Society Newton International Fellowship at Cambridge University, and elected to a Research Fellowship at Hughes Hall, Cambridge, where he also obtained a MA. He is currently leading the processing and prototyping group at the Istituto Italiano di Tecnologia (IIT), Graphene Labs. He was responsible in defining the ten years scientific and technological roadmap for the European Graphene Flagship. He is now Deputy of the workpackage Innovation of the Flagship. He was featured as 2016 Emerging Investigator by J. Mater. Chem. A and in 2019 by ChemPlusChem. His research interests encompass both the fundamental understanding and solution processing of novel nanomaterials and their technological applications. He is Co-founder of BeDimensional Spa.

 	Fig. 55  Emission decay time vs. temperature for microcrystalline [Cu(dmp)(phanephos)](PF6), 363, excited at λexc = 355 nm (pulse width 7 ns). The emission was detected at λdet = 550 nm. The solid line represents a fit of bi-exponential decay model to the experimental data with a phosphorescence decay time τ(T1) = 240 μs, at 77 K. The resulting fit parameters are ΔE(S1–T1) = 1000 cm−1 and τ(S1) = 40 ns, representing the magnitude of the singlet–triplet splitting and the decay time of the spontaneous S1 → S0 fluorescence, respectively. The spontaneous fluorescence is not observed directly due to much faster intersystem crossing. τ(DF) = 14 μs which is the decay time of the delayed fluorescence at ambient temperature. Reproduced from ref. 449 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry, copyright 2013.
